meal she left the house. Her body was
found at 6 o'clock this evening, cold in

on the place. She had fastened a hayrope
to the beam, and, placing* a i'noose" a bout
her neck, had stepped off a "pile of shin
gles on which she stood. Apparently she
slowly strangled. Coroner McDonald has
taken charge of the body and will hold an
inquest.

One of the European observatories is at
Mount Ventoux. in Provence, France.
The inountaln, -which is one of the last
ramifications of the Alps, rises In a pyra
midal mass to the height of 6230 feet and
gives a superb view of the panorama of
the Rhone. The use of this high and iso
lated summit, was proposed by the Mete
orological Commission of the Department
of Vaucluse in 1S79, and national and local
subscriptions were solicited for the erec
tion of a building which should benefit
meteorology, including weather and flood
predictions. It was necessary to first con
struct a road twelve miles long to unite
the summit of the mountain with the
thoroughfare beiow. In 1S83 the observa
tory was opened . (or meteorological work.
There is a main building 100x35 feet, which
is sunk In the north slope of the summit,
having its principal facade to the south.
On the first floor Is the dwelling and of
fice- of the meteorological observer. On
the summit of the mountain is a seml-cir
cular platform, on which are placed me
teorological Instruments. Six observations
are made daily. The observatory coat
about $50,000.

VENERABLE, revered for his no
bility of character and honored
for his achievements in the realm
of pure science. Dr. H. W. Hark
ness went peacefully to his long
rest in the gray dawn of yesterday morn
ing. His death occurred in his apart
ments at the Pacific-Union " Club, where
he had been confined to his bed since
early in June.
The aged scientist's eightieth birthday.
May 25, was an occasion of celebration
among his most intimate friends. With
in a week afterward the feebleness of old
age compelled him to take to bed, and he
failed steadily from that time until death
ensued.
F. J. Harkness of Skofleld. Utah, a
r.ephew and the only surviving near re
lative, was with Dr. Harkness during his
last moments. IJfelong friends did all
that was possible during the venerable
physician's last illness to add to his com
fort and happiness.
Dr. Harkness was one of the most emi
nent scientists of which California has been
able to boast, and in his chosen specialty.
In botany, the lower cryptogams, he -was
for many years an authority. His name
has been closely associated with the Cali
fornia Academy of Sciences for more
than a quarter of a century, and a very
considerable portion of the success of that
institution has been due to his unselfish
devotion to the cause of science.
The funeral services will be held on Sat
urday at 11 o'clock at the chapel. Odd
Allows* Cemetery.
Scientific Bent in Youth-
Karvey Wilson Harkness was born May
25. 1E21. at Pelham. Mass. His parents
were of Scottish birth. As the seventh
child of a large family in comparatively
humble circumstances his educatjonal op
portunities were limited, but he early dis
played a. fondness for study and perse
verance in the pursuit of knowledge. Â¦ He
turned his attention to the study of medi
cine and In 1817 was graduated from the
Berkshire Medical College:
The young physician joined a party of
emigrants at Rock Island, Illinois, and
Journeyed across the plains to California.
They met Â¦with many hardships but finally
crossed the Sierras Into the land of gold
in October of 1849.
Dr. HarkneEs at flret located at Bid-
The services rendered by Dr. Harkness
to' the Academy of Sciences, the Geo
graphical Society and other scientific'
bodies of the city were of â¢ Inestimable
value. Being a . man of : independent
means, of scientific attainments, broad
experience, and without, other interests
to encrross his . attention, he was able to
Inestimable Service to Science.
Dr. Harkness' researches during his
first years of residence in San Francisco
began, to attract attention. In 1S75 he be
came connected with the Academy of Sci
ences and the study of fungi became his
hobby. His devotion to science and his
unremitting efforts to advance the Inter
ests of the Academy, were recognized Â¦ in
18S7 by his election as president of that
scientific body. He served in that-capa
city until 1896. when he withdrew and
was succeeded by Dr. David Starr Jor
dan. ';' . " :
When the first transcontinental railroad
was completed Dr. Harkness presented on
behalf of the State of California the gold
6pikes which were driven by Leland
Stanford, May 10, 1S69. On May 17 of the
same year Dr. Harkness was present as
the invited guest o'f the Viceroy of Egypt
at the opening of the Suez Canal.
Dr. Harkness removed to San Francisco
In 1869 and resided here continuously from
that time until his death. He was a great
traveler, and made many trips to Europe
and to the Eastern cities and two visits
to North Africa. ; " v J^V"
Hemoves to San Francisco.
In 1854 Dr. Harkness married Miss Ame
lia Grtswold. She lived less than a year
after their marriage, and her death was a
blow that lefY a scar which time did not
efface.
Dr. Harkness was chosen in 1853 as
president of Sacramento's first Board of
Education.
wells Bar and began the practice of his
profession there. A year later he had
come to realize the larger opportunities
that were offered at Sacramento and he
removed to that bustling mining camp.
He met with a high degree of success, be
coming one of the best known physicians
of the State. He amassed a considerable
fortune and in 1S69 he retired permanent
ly from the practice' of medicine and
thenceforth devoted himself to scientific
research and the furtherance of the work
and interests of scientific societies.
Dr.\ Harkness' scientific research was
in the field of the lower cryptogamsâ
chiefly a study of the fungi of the Pacific
'Coast. He was the author, of a large
number of bulletins and monographs pub
lished by the Academy of Sciences, 'and
was a frequent contributor 'to scientific
journals. His collection of cryptogams,
containing 10,000 specimens, than which
none finer exists in any museum, was pre
sented by him to the Academy of Sciences.

Secretary Wilson of the Department of
Agriculture has expressed his approval of
the ; plan, long entertained i by Alexander
McAdie, local forecast official of the
Weather Bureau In SanFrancIsco, for. the
establishment of a high class station,' ob
servatory and . laboratory on the summit
of Tamalpais Mountain. He has also re
quested Forecaster McAdie to prepare
estimates for the cost, which Is roughly
fixed at about < $200,000. ..' The -Department
of Agriculture has not the funds, so. Mc-
Adie says, to establish the proposed scien
tlflcjplant at this time. V; He Is preparing
the estimates- as. requested. He thinks
that there are wealthy men In the country
who could well afford to build for them
selves this striking scientific^ monument to
their memory, on- the,, towering peak of
old ' Tamalpals. The Interest that Sec
retary Wilson Â¦. has manifested is signifi
cant. If it should lead to results in the
near future It would mean that the fore
casting for this region ? would be per
formed at the summit and not In San
Francisco. . ,
It would also bring about an extension
of the study of meteorological phenomena
on a wider scale than has been possible.
Forecaster McAdie* thinks that the ob
servatory and laboratory should be, the
best and most complete in the world.
! When the Presidential party was in San
Francisco Secretary 'Wilson had several
conversations with- McAdie on the sub
ject of the observatory on Mount Tamal
pias. â¢ * ;
_ -Weather Breeding Topography.
The Secretary, stood on the summit of
Mount .Tamalpals and viewed the won
derful topography* of the country about
the ' bayjofy- Sao . -^i'anoisco, ~ the A Golden
Gate, the mountain ranges on both 6ldes
of tho bay and the valleys "that, are re
vealed from the mountain top and he was
greatly interested. To the west was the
broad expanse of the Pacific ' Ocean,
over which sweep the storms, that strike
the Pacific Coast as their initial point of
visitation in the territory of the United
States on their journeys eastward. North,
south and east, for many leagues, until
the view was bounded by the horizon, ex
tended, the fair land of California, with
vast agricultural interests to be benefited
by prompt knowledge of the meteorologi
cal disturbances from the sea. Almost at
his feet, as it appeared, was the city of
San Francisco, which manifest destiny
has selected to be one of the great com
mercial ports of the world. . He realized
that the summit of Tamalpais was favor
ably situated for the study of the
meteorological phenomena of the levels
from 1500 to 4000 feet and that It was feas
ible there to study frost, formations of
fog and other phenomena, which, in their
final application,- are of great practical
concern to mankind. What will come of
the matter is yet uncertain. Forecaster
McAdie insists that the department Is not
prepared to take action. There are
others who believe that the Secretary has
not Invited the preparation of estimates
out of mere idleness and curiosity.
Four Buildings Required.
To make up the complete plant required,
will, in McAdie's ppinion, require one cen
tral building and three other structures.
The central building would be the seat of
weather observations for this region. The
fitness of Mount Tamalpais for meteoro
logical observation has already, been de
termined by the small station placed on
the mountain some years ago at the in
stance of the forecaster. In two of the
other buildings there would be instru
ments, etc., for spectroscopic, optical and
electrical work. There would be uses
not defined for the third building.
"The problem before us," said the.
weather forecaster, j "Is the successful
scientific forecasting of atmospheric con
ditions. In no other direction would the
work of the aero-physical laboratory be
so Â¦ pronounced. Carried from short pe
riods-to periods of weeks and months,
what 'branch of applied will be
found to exercise so great an influence
upon the r welfare of man? There is a
popular 'impression that forecasting
weather i conditions is, in high degree, a
matter of scientific procedure. . Much that
is scientific- in character has been done,"
but, without disparaging such work, the
present condition of our. knowledge Is
quite ; unsatisfactory. The direction in
which the demand, for^immediate appli
cation of our knowledge of aero-physics is
greatest Is in. connection with the tides
and fluxes of that aerial ocean in which
we live, the storms and currents of the
atmosphere.' We know almost nothing of
the electrification of the atmoaphet e.
How, the air receives its charge .we Tlo
not know and of the distribution of the
potential and the significance of its varia
tions we have only fragmentary and scant
knowledge." ' 'Â¦â¢- * Â¦- h -' -

GOVERNOR SEES
A BULLFIGHT
Sport of Spaniards En
joyed by Nebraska's
Executive.'
â¢ Special Dispatch to The* Call.
OMAHA, Nebr., July 10.â The South
Omaha Fair to-day celebrated Governor's
day, and a special bullfight was provided
for the executive's entertainment. A half
dozen animals were Introduced, but each
was listless in the intense heat and the
show was tame. One huge animal sought
shelter in a shady spot and the perform
ers prodded it' with their blunt lances In
vain.
The exhibition was followed by wild
bronco breaking, which was much more
acceptable in the Governor's sight, the ex
ecutive being himself a frontiersman.
Previous to the bullfight there ocurred
an elaborate parade through the avenues
cf the fair, companies of the Tenth In
fantry and various militia organizations
participating. Governor Savage was at
tended by his staff in full regalia. . . ;
In a published interview to-day Gover
nor Savage indorsed the sport of bull
fighting, providing the animal itself es
caped harm. He was told that so far no
bull's blood had been shed and replied:
"That's good. So long as the bull is not
badly treated there is no ground for in
tervention. The performers go into the
arena with a : full knowledge of the risk
involved, and if they are hurt it Is their
own lookout." '
I The bullfights will take place daily dur
ing the progress of the fair. It Is ru
mored that the last fight on Saturday
night will be for blood and the humane
society is making a special Inquiry in con
sequence. riÂ£ ~ : ' 'Â¦'..' Â¦

The nomination of Colonel Kilbourne
was appropriately called one of "spon
taneous combustion." Howell, Hidy, Al
shire and Holman were favorites at great
odds frcm the start. Young was sprung
as a surprise and nominated over Smoots,
who had been such a favorite that others
would not previously enter the race for
Clerk of the Supreme Court. None of the
delegates outside of Cleveland knew
Young, and he was nominated because
Cuyahoga wanted him. Cuyahoga could
get anything it wanted, except the head
of the State ticket, after the Johnson
amendments were ingrafted into the Mc-
Lean platform, and that document as
amended was adopted with equanimity.
Defeat of Monnett.
It was currently reported that Johnson
desired the nomination of Monnett as At
torney General, and the McLean men
wanted ilonnett defeated. At any rate,
the defeat of Monnett was so decisive as
to be a feature of the convention, and he
was not defeated by Mykrantz, who had
been the favorite, but by McCarthy, who
was being called an "also ran" on the
floor of the convention until one of the
Monnett men circulated the report that
McCarthy was an attorney for the Stan
dard Oil Company. When the reaction
came on this charge. Mykrantz withdrew
during the ballot, in favor of McCarthy,
and the latter-got two-thirds of the votes.
Monnett as Attorney General had
brought suits against the Standard Oil
Company and other corporations to cancel
their charters under the anti-trust law.
He had sought a third term nomination
from the Republicans on the issue that
he should be retained in that office to
continue these prosecutions. After his de
feat for the third* nomination in 1899. he
Eupported Bryan in 1900 on the anti-trust
issue and cam'assed Ohio and other State*
with Brjran. Jud^e Smalley In present
ing Monaett's name said he was known
from the Golden Gate, San Francisco, to
the Heir Gate, Xew York, as the enemy
of trusts.
Bryan ? s Picture Is Trampled.
In the alleged -stampede "for principles
rather than men" the most striking turn
of the convention was on Bryan. The
most bitter things were said on his leader
ship this morning when It was said that
his name should not be mentioned, and
that there should be no reference to cither
of the national platforms on which he
made his campaigns. After this plan had
been agreed on one of the twenty-one
members of that committee offered a
minority report reaffirming the Kansas
City platform and expressing confidence
in Bryan, and he received only six votes
out of 950 delegates on his substitute for
the platform preamble. A few moments
after the platform was adopted one of
these six delegates called attention to the
fact that pictures of other Democrats
were displayed in the hall and none of
Bryan, as heretofore. He started, to carry
a small banner with Bryan's picture on it
to the platform. The aisles were ordered
cleared and the picture did not reach its
destination. It was trampled under foot
and spoiled during the wild demonstration
when Kilbourne was escorted into the
hall. Members of the committee on reso
lutions say that over one hour was spent
after last midnight in efforts to strike the
word "salable" out of the plank on rail
way taxation, and several hours in de
nouncing the Chicago and Kansas C'ty
platforms and the leader who stood- on
them. The platform caused much com
ment to-night over what it does not tay
as well as what it does say.

CINCINNATI, July 10.â The twentieth
international convention of Christian En
deavorers came to a close to-day.. Audi
toriums Endeavor and Williston were
used for two big rallies in the morning
devoted to a discussion of the twentieth
anniversary session of the society. Secre
tary John Willis Baer of Boston presided
In Auditorium Endeavor. George W. Cole
man of Boston described the Christian
Endeavor work among soldiers and sail
ors. He said that two went down with the
Maine in Havana harbor and that twelve
more sailed with Dewey and the Olympia
into Manila. Bay. The Rev. George W.
Moore of Nashville, Tenn., told of the
work among the Afro-Americans, and
Frederick A. Wallace of Lexington, Ky.,
described the prison work of the Endeav
orers. The Rev. J. F. Cowan" of Boston
dwelt on the work among the foreigners
in this country. - -
Bishop B. W. Arnett' of Wilberforce,
Ohio, presided at the meeting at Audito
rium Williston. "What the Church Has
Gained"-- was the- subject -treated -by -the

: The afternoon rallies were given over to

needs.: President Clark presided at the
meeting In Auditorium Endeavor. Robert
E. Speer of New York, a member of the

the injunction of the Savior to teach the
gospel, in all lands. The Rev. Willis P.

thrilling story of the establishment of his
mission in the African jungles.
The evils of opium and liquor In mis
sionary fields were given a- scoring. The
Rev. G. L. Wharton of Hiram, Ohio, a
missionary, said that they were the great
est evils in the missionary fields, because
the cheapest, strongest and most madden
ing kinds, of. liquors were used by the
masses of- heathen. The Rev. J. P. Jones
of India stated that for many centuries
India was a sober nation, till the coming
of the Anglo-Saxon with his drinks, and
that to-day drunkenness was regarded by
manj' as a mark of distinction. If Eng
land should evacuate India â¢ to-day, he
said, the greatest evidence of her occu
pancy would be the number of empty
whisky bottles. Edward Holten of India
corroborated these statements.

"WASHINGTON, July 10.â The State De
partment has received the amount of the
An.erican indemnity for the claim against
Turkey, $95,000, through the American le
gation at Constantinople.
The money was paid by the Turkish
Government to Mr. Leishman, our Minis
ter at Constantinople, and was by him
placed in the Ottoman Imperial Bank and
drafts remitted for the amount. These
drafts have just reached Washington. As
is always the case, the claims in the ag
gregate considerably exceed the amount
of the indemnity actually paid, but our
Government has expressed itself satisfied
with the payment. It assumes full respon
sibility for the distribution, the Turkish
Government paying down aÂ«lump sum of
$95,000, and leaving It to the State Depart
ment to distribute it among the claim
ants at its discretion and after its own
fashion. It is stated that , as soon as the
department officials can prepare their list
it will communicate directly with the
claimants. . .
These claims are principally based on
losses suffered by the American mission
ary and educational institutions in Tur
key,, notably those at Harpoot and Ma
rash, but there are a number of Individ
ual claims, such, for instance, as that of
the family of the unfortunate cyclist
Lenz, the Pittsburg man who was killed
by Turkish soldiers while attempting to
go around the globe on his wheel.
â¢ The State Department officials feel the
greatest satisfaction at the settlement of
these claims. Secretary Hay has been
told by diplomats skilled in the ways of
Oriental diplomacy and experienced in the
political conditions of Southern Europe
that he never would be able to collect
them. Among other difficulties the State
Department had to contend with was the
jealousy of the great European powers,
mest of whom had claims .against Tur
key vastly larger In amount than ours,
and whose total was beyond the ability
of the Turkish Government to meet. For
more than a decade the American claims
have been pending. .

Opposes Boundary Extension.
Our merchant marine to be restored to its
former greatness and made the ally of the peo
ple against monopoly by the repeal of antl
quate and restricted navigation laws. No sub
sidies for favored shipowners.
Tariff reform was never more urgently de
manded than now. when th*e existence and
abuses of trusts have been brought about by
the policy of protection of favored industries.
The abolition ,of the so-called protective sys
tem and the substitution in Its place of the
traditional Democratic policy of a tariff for
revenue so levied as not to burden one indus
try for the benefit of another is demanded.
The enactment and the vigorous enforcement
of measures which shall prevent all monopolies
and combinations in restraint of trade and com
merce â the matter of first importance being: to
prevent the use of the Government as an in
strumentality for the creation and increase of
the wealth of the few. while preserving intact
the rlgrht of private property and the fullest
measure of individual liberty of contract and
assuring to every man the Just reward of su
perior industry and skill. We demand the sup
pression of all trusts and a return to industrial
freedom. As a means to that end all trust
products should be placed on the freÂ«S list and
the Government should exercise a more -rigid
supervision of transportation lines and abolish
In fact all unjust discrimination in rates.
The reserved rights of the State and people
to be upheld. Centralization, which would de
stroy them, to be condemned. A strict con
struction of the constitution. The greatest in
dividual liberty consistent with public order
and welfare.
The present Republican administration, in
the conduct of public affairs and in the man
agement of public institutions, has been the
most costly in the history of the State.
Benevolent and penal institutions should be
conducted upon a non-partisan and business
basis..
Tariff Reform Demanded.
The proceedings of the Republican majority
of the State Board of Equalization are a scan
dal. Property values, instead of being equal
ized, were increased or diminished at the dic
tation of political bosses, pursuant to corrupt
combinations and conspiracies. . , .
Steam and electric railways and other cor
porations possessing public franchises shall be
assessed in the, same proportion to their sal
able value as are farm and city real estate.
The Acceptance of free passes or other favors
from railroads by public officers or employes
shall be made adequate ground for vacating
the offices held by them.
The government of municipalities of the
State should be radically reformed' and wise
general laws be- enacted to correct abuses and
prevent their recurrence. - ',
The Democratic party, therefore, calls upon
the people to unite with it in placing the con
duct of municipal affairs upon a business basis,
that they may be administered by tha people
in the interest of all the people on the prin
ciple of home rule. No franchise, extension or
renewal thereof ever to be granted by any city
or village without first submitting the same to
a vote of Â¦ the people.

agricultural machinery. There is as yet
no agriculture in Manchuria, and not
enough in Siberia to justify the importa
tion of machinery. The steamers will
bring to America teas, silks, dried fish,
gold, copper, coal and tobacco. If Man
churia is ever opened to settlement the
lumbering industry willalso grow to enor
mous proportions, for there are vast for
ests there which have never been touched.
More than this, there is the trade of
Europe, much of which will eventually
come this way. But it will be seen that
for many years to come, at all events, the
balance of trade between San Francisco
and Siberia and Russian Manchuria must
be in favor of this port.
The steamers of the Russian line al
ready built and those building are large,
modern vessels of the most approved pat
tern, and will make competition on the
Pacific of the keenest. Running as they
will under the flag and with the protection
of the Russian Government, the competi
tion will be in fact as well as in name,
and the struggle for commercial su
premacy between the Russian city and the
English and Japanese ports ln the Orient
will be a death grapple from the first.
The merchants of San Francisco will do
well to keep their attention upon the new
Russian city of Dalny. When the Czar
builds a city he sees that it becomes
great, and the building of Dalny is a part
of the Russian plan to win unquestioned
supremacy in Northern Asia.
it Â¦will one day be enormous in volume â
will pass through Dalny. The manufac
tures of the United States destined to
supply the colonists on the steppes of Si
beria will be received and transhipped at
Dalny. and from Dalny the products of
the Old World will be shipped to San
Francisco for distribution to all parts of
America. The trade that has already be
gun to find its T7ay to -Vladivostok will
eventually â¢ be centered at the new and
more southerly port of Dalny, where there
will be no freezing of the sea to block
commerce in winter.
It will be from Dalny that the new
steamship line between San Francisco and
the Russian possessions in Asia 'will run.
The company that is to run these steam
ers, a Russian corporation, is already in
existence and is pushing out boats for
trade along the shores of China. One of
the boats that is destined for the trans
pacific trade is completed. A second wai
launched last month. Eight more have
been contracted for and will be built as
speedily as possible. The first of these
boats to cross the Pacific will reach the
port of San Francisco in December next,
and after that the boats will make regu
lar trips.

SOME Idea of the character of the
work the Russians are doing at
Port Arthur may be gathered trom
the picture that accompanies this
article, the first photograph from
the neighborhood of the Czar's great fort
ress en the Chinese coast that has ever
been printed in this country- Probably it
Is the first photograph ever taken of Port
Arthur. The officers of the Russian Gov
ernment do not lend any great degree of
encouragement to the kodak idea, partic
ularly in the vicinity of defensive works.
The picture was obtained by a gentleman
who passed through that region lately
and is the choicest ln his collection. It
shows the outer harbor, and is taken from
the railroad " wharf . On the bluff to the
right are the great defensive works of the
port, built by the Russians and destined
to make Port Arthur ln effect impregna
ble. Within this bluff and still to the
right lies the Inner harbor, while the
basin and the Government docks are to
the left.
It is deemed necessary to thus expialn
the photograph both because it is the first
ever printed and because the people of
San Kranclsco have more than a. passing
Interest in the work the Russian Govern
ment is now doing at Port Arthur and all
through Manchuria. It will be borne in
mind that at the close of the Chinese-
Japanese war, when the forces of the
Mikado captured the Chinese stronghold
of Weihaiwei, there was a sudden and
startling collapse of the Peking Govern
ment and the world saw that the Mon
golian giant was the veriest weakling
among the nations. Then came the treaty
of peace negotiated between the plenipo
tentiaries of the powers and the cession
of Manchuria to Japan ln default of a
war indemnity.
The Sand of Russia.
It was at this point that the White Czar
showed his hand. It was no part of the
Russian plan that the control of Man
churia should pass to any power other
than the Muscovite. Manchuria was the
key to the Russian situation in Eastern
Asia. So long as China held Manchuria
and .China was strong the Czar would
make no move. With China known to be
weak it was not to be tolerated that a
strong power should enter that province
and hold the Russian forever locked ln
the Ice of Siberia. The great trans-Si
berian Railway, pushing out from. Mos
cow and St. Petersburg to Vladivostok,
would find the sea and justify itself both
as a commercial enterprise and as a
strategic movement, but it woiild find the
sea, with Manchuria in hostile hands, at
a point that would be locked in ice during
at least half the year. Russia could never
realize that supremacy in the Northern
Pacific, of which "all Russian statesmen
dream, with only the port of Vladivostok
as a marine base. The Russian vision has
been of a port below the freezing zone.
The prowess of Japan gave the Russians
their opportunity. The war and the out
rages of the Boxers made that oppor
tunity complete. When Japan seized Wei
haiwei Russia coolly seized Port Arthur,
and the Chinese Government connived at
the seizure. The reason for that became
apparent later. The treaty of peace nego
tiated with Japan, giving to that country
practical possession of the province of
Manchuria, was no sooner promulgated
than the St. Petersburg Government
stepped to the front with the announce
ment that Russia could not look with in
difference upon this practical dismember
ment of the Chinese empire "in a quarter
that had always been considered within

Russia, unknown to the world, la build
ing a great port on the gulf of Talienwan,
almost under tho guns of Port Arthur
Forty miles in> a northwesterly direction
from that fortress, on the shore of the
gulf of Talienwan and with a harbor that
is said by expert engineers to be one of
the best and most commodious In the
â¢world, the Russians have laid the founda
tions of their new city. It is called Dal
ny. The name is Russian and the plans
will be Russian. The siteâ for the build-
Ing of the city has already begunâ is on
the line of the Manchuria Railway. Dal
ny will be, in fact, the commercial ter
minus of that line and the point from
which Russian enterprise will seek to con
trol the commerce of the Pacific. There
will be great business houses there, and
from that port the steamers that are to
carry the Russian flag to all the western
world will clear. Tho vast trade from
America to Europe through Siberiaâ and
Eus3ia's New City.
But in the seizure of Port Arthur the
whole Russian scheme was not accom
plished. That gave a military and naval
base. The Russians are a modern ag
gressive people, and they realize therefore
that it is also necessary to secure com
mercial supremacy in the Northern Pa
cific. Port Arthur gives them the mili
tary advantage Manchuria gives that
military base unbroken communication by
land with the capital of the Muscovite
empire over Russian territory. It Is neces
sary to found a great commercial entrepot
with a trade that will ramify to all the
points oÂ» the shores of the great ocean
at the doors of Russia and America. It
is here that the vital Interest of San
Francisco in the movements of Russia
comes in, for it is with this future Rus
sian metropolis that the commerce of San
Francisco will be carried on.
Then came the concession for a Russian
railway through Manchuria from Mukden
to Port Arthur, giving an outlet for the
trans-Siberian line south of the freezing
zone. That was a long step, although the
railway still ran through Chinese terri
tory. Not until the Boxer outbreak did
the Russians complete the work that was
probably In the minds of the St. Peters
burg Cabinet when Port Arthur was
seized. The relief of the legationers at
Peking saw at its close the' Russians In
actual armed possession of the whole of
Manchuria, and saw, too, the expressed
purpose of the Czar to hold what he had
taken, despite the protest of all the world
In arms. The world in arms weakened
and the Czar holds what he has. In the
dispatches of yesterday the announcement
was made that China had resolved to
withdraw all Its troops from Manchuria
"to avoid the possibility of collision with
the Russians." This may not be an actual
cession of territory in form, but it an
swers the same purpose. The Russians
have the province.
Russia and the Boxers.
the sphere of Russian Influence." The
Mikado did not deem himself as strong
then as he probably does now, and Man
churia was abandoned. It was said at the
time by the diplomatic â¢world that China
had paid Port Arthur as the price of the
interference of Russia. It w.as known
then and has become more apparent since
that Russian diplomacy has made the rep
resentative of the Czar at Peking- the
most influential of all the European diplo
mats accredited to the court of the Man
chu Emperor. The Russian Embassador
was listened to when any other European
Minister who approached the forbidden
city took his life in his hands. And the
prestige of England, which Russia had
most gravely to fear in the Far East, was
destroyed almost at one blow.
Ministers preach and physicians prac
tice.

Lynchcrs Are Sorry Now.

out the child on the -wrist. Mr. Kinney,

Kinney. his wife was well pleased with
Yung Fook as a cook, but a few days ago
he had threatened to leave, but as hi3
salary was raised $5 a month he consented
to remain. Yesterday afternoon Mrs.

Fook. who had acted peculiarly, without
Training lung-ed at Mrs. Kinney viciously

CROWDS WAIT
AT THE BORDER
Trouble was prevented by the early an
nouncement that the line organization
would be respected by the Government of
ficers. Cheers and waving of hats greeted
the word and from this time on there was
no sign of trouble. Applicants were ad
mitted to the booths four at a time and
the filing proceeded rapidly all day long.
' During the day the heat became intense,
but no serious suffering was reported. The
numerous women in line were treated gal
lantly by the men, who shaded them from
the sun with umbrellas and furnished
drinks from the lemonade venders.
The second place of registration named
in the proclamation was at Lftwton, twen
ty-five miles overland, where r similar
scenes to those enactett in El Reno were
witnessed. The people in the vicinity; of
Mountain View and other points along the
border contiguous to that point left yes
terday for i the interior' and presented
themselves early to-day at. the Lawton
booths. ... .,, - > ...
Cheers From the Multitude.
tiersman, sprawled out in the diist. The
crowd before each booth elected a cap
tain and each man and woman in line was
given a number, which they pinned con
spicuously to their clothes. A company
member was permitted occasionally to ab
sent, himself fromthe line -for a short
breathing spell, and Invariably his place
was protected by his fellow watchers.
As the hour of 9 o'clock neared inter
lopers tried to push in and break the
numerical- order in the line organization.
This Instantly raised bad blood, and when
word was passed down the line a little
later that the booth officials would ' not
recognize the line organization but would
register the first persons to present them
selves there were threats of violence and
rioting seemed likely.

prizes are to be distributed. Foremost
among them is William Hayes, king of
American riflemen. On many, a range he
has earned medals and prizes. Accom
panying him are .Fred C. Rose, the first
king of the National Federation of Shoot
ers; . H.'-M. Pope, the celebrated Connec
ticut sharpshooter; Messrs.- Busfield,
Geisel and Hansen, all famous for the
accuracy of their aim and the accomplish
ment of feats .with the rifle that have won
for them honor and substantial reward.

comers are making the best of the short
time which is left before the fest is in
stalled. On the Shell Mouhd ranges they
are to be found. The change of range,
atmospheric and light conditions are be
ing studied by the enthusiasts and the
candidates for prize honors.
The . prize of the.New York Schuetzen
Corps, the oldest in America, arrived yes
terday. It is $150 in bright, newly minted
gold arranged artistically In a handsome
plush case.
Captain F. A'. Kuhls, president of the
National Federation of Clubs, accompa
nied the reception committee on the way
to meet the distinguished visitors. On
the committee were John D. Heise, Louis
Haake, August Jungblut, N. Ahrens, F.
P. Schuster, Henry M. Wreden, Edward
H. Goetze and George Alpers.
Two 'days after the opening of the fest,
which will be on. July 14, the Native Sons
will have a day. The entertainment com
mittee of the bund received a communi
cation yesterday from Henry Lunstedt,
grand secretary of the Native Sons, in
forming the committee that the board of
officers of the Grand Parlor had formally
accepted the designation of July 16 as
Native Sons' day.
Mr. C. George Krogness of Chicago, whai
Is a special advertising agent represent*
Ing several of the leading dallies of the
United States, has for several weeks been,
suffering from serious complications re
sulting from overwork, and accordingly
left Chicago about a week ago for a trip
to California.
He was provided with transportation,
which he carried In a pocket memoran
dum book, and leaving bis affairs In com
petent hands started on bis journey. His
route took him over the Burlington to
Denver, the Denver and Rio Grande and
the Rio Grande and "Western to Ogderi
and the Southern Pacific to San Fran
cisco.
As a rule in his travels Mr. Krogness,
possessed of a very affable disposition,
converses freely with the different con
ductors en route, but on this trip, feellns
too weak to talk, he lay Idly back; on the
Pullman cushions and handed up his
pecketbook to whatever conductor ap
peared without looking to see if the tick
ets were properly punched or the coupons
regularly torn off. He noticed, however,
that each conductor scrutinized his face
intently and quizzically, then smiled and
passed on without comment.
His serenity was undisturbed until near
ing Sixteenth street, Oakland, -where the
ferry tickets are given out. Mr. Krog
ness failed to receive his and asked the
conductor for one. The latter laughed
and reulied: "That ticket of yours is
good on any train in the United States,
old boy. but I can't Issue transfers on it
without getting into trouble."
"Why, what do you mean?" exclaimed
Krogness. "Aren't my tickets regular?"
And he pulled out his pocketbook.
All that he found there was a. miniature
reproduction of the well-known "hold-up'*
Gold Dust poster which adorns the bill
boards of every city In the country.
He was simply bereft of speech for the
nonce, then lie exclaimed: "You don't
mean to tell me that I have traveled from
Chicago across the continent on an ad
vertisement of Gold Dust washing pow
der, do you?"
"That's what you have." replied tho
man of the punch. "There Isn't a con
ductor In the brotherhood who doesn't
consider 'Gold Dust' worth Its weight In
gold and anybody who has the passport
doesn't need to worry about being put off.
the train."
Mr. Krogness found his other tickets re
posing in hla grip and la keeping tha
punched poster ticket as a souvenir.

FOG PREVENTS
BIG YACHT RACE
Third Meeting Between
Ninety:, Footers De
j "., * clajred Off. .
Start Is Made, but Boats Are
Soon Obscured From
View.
NEWPORT, R. I..' July 10.â The . third
race between the Constitution, Columbia
and Independence came to an inglofious
end late this afternoon In a dead calm
and thickest kind of Atlantic fog. There
was little time to form judgment on the.
relative speed of the three boats, for in
less than half an hour after the start the
boats ran into the fog and at no time
after that could all three be seen at one
time. ,.
A little over an hour after the start the
Constitution withdrew and came back to
the start. The Columbia and Indepen
dence stood on for the outer mark, which
they rounded with the Columbia about a
mile in the lead. The wind, however, died
down almost to a calm and the leading
boat could not finish in the time limit.
The race was therefore off and will be
sailed over to-morrow. At the timo the
Constitution withdrew, she was leading
the Columbia in the beat to windward by
nearly a mile and the Independence by
nearly two*miles. .
SUPERVISORS SIT
AS EQUALIZERS
Several Small Property-
Owners Secure Lower
Assessments.
The Board of Supervisors met as a
Board of Equalization yesterday after
noon and proceeded to consider the appli
cations- of many small and a few large
property owners for reductions in their
assessments. Mayor Phelan was not pres
ent when the meeting opened, although
he dropped in later, and Supervisor Mc-
Carthy took the chair.
Alexander W. Wilson was denied a re
duction in his assessment of property at
Turk and Taylor streets from $40,000 to
$20,000, and R. J. Techau, who wanted a
$10,000 reduction on a $20,000 assessment,
was allowed $5000.
Then Daniel Meyer, capitalist, stood up
before the board and was sworn. He was
asked what reduction he wanted.
--Â¦ "I- don't want any. said Meyer. â¢ "I
don't want to be raised."
"How much were you assessed for?"
asked Supervisor McCarthy.
"Five hundred and eighty thousand dol
larF." . . â¢-
"Did you apply for a reduction?" â¢ â¢
"No; I was void to come up, so I would
not be raised." _;
"Ah," said McCarthy.- - "Your '. assess-'
ment was arbitrary?" .- . . .
"That is so." . ._
"And was it just? Have you that "much
personal property?" . . , ', -
"I cannot say. Possibly less; possibly
more.. I do not know- how much. But I
do not want it raised."
"Let us hear from the Assessor," sug
gested McCarthy.
"I object." said Supervisor Stafford.
"The Assessor should not say anything
until Meyer justifies himself."
"How '.can I justify ' myself ?" asked
Meyer." -;
"You have not given a proper answer tc
the question," replied Siafford.
Does Not Know How Wealthy He Is.
"I cannot give any other answer. I do
not know how much I am worth."
"But we want to know," Stafford insist
ed. "We would like to raise you if we
could. >
"That is a bad spirit," said Meyer,
sadly.
Then he went away, and the Mayor,
who came in at the moment and bowed
to Aley<$r, , observed, solto voce, "A- case
of Daniel in the lions' den."
The board reduced the assessment of
the Pacific Glass Works to a merely nom
inal figure because the old plant had been
abandoned.
H. H. Wyckoff, representing the Congre
gational Church Building Society of New
York, asked to be relieved of the pay
ment of taxes on a mortgage for $1403
given by Bethlehem Congregational
Church to the people he represents, be
cause the money had been borrowed to
build a church and 'was in a sense a char
itable donation, inasmuch as no interest
was charged on the loan. ...
"It makes no real difference to us,"
Wyckoff said, "because if the mortgage is
taxed the congregation will feel in honor
bound to pay the taxes."
Raises a New Point.
The point raised was a new one. "But,"
said the Mayor, "how far can the board
take cognizance of the benevolent, inten
tion of mortgagees? We do not dispute
you, but we are not exempting from txa
ation money lenders who advance money
to churches with the expectation of get
ting their interest in heaven. If you had
taken a mere note from Bethlehem con
gregation, trusting to its honor to repay
you, we -could 'not have levied a tax." .
The application after some discussion
was rejected.
The balance of the session was devoted
to the equalization of small assessments,
almost every application made. for reduc
tion being granted.
The list of arbitrary assessments was
then taken up, together with the names
of those property owners who had been
notified but who had failed to appear. Of
these latter all reductions 'Â¦ recommended
by the Assessor were ordered made. The
others, with the list of arbitrary assess
ments, went over for consideration until
next Friday at 2:30 p. m., to which time
the board adjourned.
Would Evade Franchise Tax.
Wells, Fargo & Co. filed a petition yes
terday with the Board of Supervisors for
a reduction of the assessment on its fran
chise of .The petition recites that
the secretary of the company made re
turn to the Assessor on June 11 as to the
personal property owned by the company,
and Assessor Dodge placed an assessment
thereon of $533,267. To this sum Dodge
added $750,000 for the company's ' fran
chise, on which no return had been made.
The company claims that its franchise is
not assessable .under the laws of the
State, and the Assessor did' not take into
account that the corporation pays taxes
in 3500 places where it has an office, and
that a higher standard of valuation was
used by the Assessor than that employed
in assessing other corporations,
j The Giant Powder Company also peti
tioned that the assessment on its franchise
of $150,000 be canceled. The company
claims to have paid $576 58 on a valuation
of $35,482 on its personal property, which
included $10,000 on .' its < franchise. Dodge
thereafter made another assessment of
$150,000 against the corporation.

FROM SEATTLE.
The hearing of the petition of Theresa
A. Oelrichs, Viriginia Vanderbilt and
Charles LÂ». Fair for distribution to them
of the personal property of' the estate
of the late James G. Fair has been turn
ed into a scramble for attorneys' fees,
with the attorneys representing the trus
tes and executors leading the scramble.
Ex-Governor Budd, Knight & Heggerty
and Charles S. Wheeler have been exam
ining Charles S. Neal, manager of the
estate, for several days to try and estab
lish the great value of the personal prop
erty which there is to distribute and 40
establish a basis for the construction of
the trust clause of the will so. that it will
fall, let loose. the personal property .into
the hands of the Fair heirs and bring big
fees to them. Attorney McEnerney ; and
ex-Judge Haynei on the other hand,
simply want to prove that the work of the
executors and their lawyers was 'well
done. They want to sustain the validity
of the trust clause If possible.
The attorneys for the executors have
already received $185,000 for their ser
vices. Under Judge Coffey's schedule
the attorneys' fees on a $17,000,000 estate,
which it is claimed the Fair estate is,
would amount to $53,000. The attorneys
have already received about four times
this amount.
On the other hand, the attorneys repre
senting the Fair heirs have not as yet
been paid. Their fees are all contingent,
so yesterday ex-Governor Budd protested
and "Wheeler protested against the effort
of opposing counsel to produce testimony
upon which to base their demand for
counsel fees, while Hayne and McEnerney
fought for the coveted right. Finally
Judge Troutt ruled that in the hearing at
bar it was not 'proper to establish a basis
for a demand in the future for attorneys'
fees, but he Initmated that in event he
ordered the distribution of the personal
property to the heirs he would order $100,
000 withheld for which the attorneys for
the trustees could battle later.
During the morning session Manager
Neal talked about millions. His, testi
mony established the following facts rela
tive to the present financial condition of
the estate:
- Total income from real estate since death,
$1,379,077 53; income sales, real estate, $150,000;
income from Pine and Sansome, $268,511 83; in
come, Mission-street property. $40,862 12; in
come from personal property, $1,887,27133; in
come from sales of personal property. $4,164.
641 80 Total repairs, $338,113 38; wheat on
hand. $30,0C0.
These figures show that the estate has
certainlv prospered . since the death of
James G. Fair and furnish a good basics
for an argument by the attorneys repre
senting the executors that their demand
for. a few hundred Â« thousand dollars is
naught but reasonable.

2JE1J7S OF THE OCEAN.
The owners of the May Flint are mak
ing good progress with the wrecking of
that vessel. They have already recov
ered 3%0 tons of her coal cargo and now
work has begun on the hull. It was at
first thought the vessel couid be raised.
but an examination showed that her back
was broken. Monday last she was blown
to pieces, and yesterday the wreckers
xt-j-re engaged in recovering portions of
the hu!l. Whatever i.= salved will be sold
for old iron, and the owners expect to
make enough out of it to pay all expenses.
Â¦Wrecking the May Flint.
Round about the foot of Third street
and infesting the hay wharf Is a gang of
"wharf rats" that is making the lives of
policemen and night watchmen miserable.
It is only a few nights since they board
ed the scow Mir.nette and heaving up the
anchor sailed her over to Oakland Creek.
They then stripped her of sails, running
gear, anchor and chain, compass and car
go covers and left her, hard and fast in
the mud. Next they ransacked the
schooner Chevalier and stole thÂ« cap
tain's clothes while he slept. In the pock
ets was the month's wages to pay the
crew, so the "rats" secured more than
S200. Eob GibEon had his Whitehall made
fast with a chain and padlock, but they
stole boat, chain, padlock and all. Hardly
a night passes but a dingey is stolen, and
the people in the vicinity are now satis
fied that nothing but a police patrol boat
will stop the thieving. San- Francisco is
the only "harbor in the world of its size
that cannot boast such a service, so it is
no wonder the "wharf rats" have it all
their own way.

Hakes a Good Bun From Hawaii,
APTAIN* LARSEN. of the schooner
if Nettie Sundborg deems his vessel
II . the fastest of the mosquito fleet
of lumber vessels. Captain Han
sen cf the S. Danielson holds the
same high opinion abo'ufHiis vessel, while
Captain Gruggel thinks the Sacramento
can beat anything of her size sailing out
of the Oolden Gate. The owners of the
vessels hold the same opinions as their
captains, so when the schooners were
ready for sea iast Friday a "three-cor
nered" race for $50 "a. corner" was ar
ranged. All three are now out six days
for Siuslaw River, and the owners of the
Nettie Sundborg expect to hear of her ar
rival early next week. Arrangements
have been made with the telegraph peo
ple and the result of the race will be sent
to the Merchants' Exchange. Ever since
the three schooners started it has been
blowing strong from the northwest, so
there will be plenty of chance for seaman
ship. It will be a dead beat to windward
all the wav up. and the chances are that
none of the vessels will reach Siuslaw by
next Monday.

The Alabama constitutional convention is said to
be, confronted by an unexpected snag; it has ex
cluded the negro from the franchise. very neatly, but

All talk about a probable commercial war between

It is announced from Washington that the Chinese
Minister Hires to have his name spelled Wu Ting-fang.
His family name is Wu, and he is proud of it. '
BY the New York Tribune there was recently
published from its Paris correspondent a de
tailed statement of the taxes imposed by the
French Government upon posters and billboard ad
vertising. Under the French law a "poster" is any
written, printed or painted placard exhibited on a
wall, boarding or other public place in order to spread
information of any kind. All- governmental posters,
whether set up by the courts or by executive or leg
islative authority, are on white paper/ while, all private
posters must be on colored paper and carry the gov
ernmental stamp.
The stamp tax varies with the size of the poster
and the population of the community. The. revenue
produced by it in 1898 was 3,515,000 francs, and in
1899 it was 3Â»553..o6o francs. The figures for 1900' were
not given by the .writer, because the income for that
year, by reason of thevexposition, was so much in
creased as not to afford a fair test of the value of the
tax as a revenue producer.
The example of France is of importance- to all
American cities that are struggling with â¢ the bill
board nuisance. It seems clear that the most "effective
way to deal with the evil would be to impose a tax
somewhat similar to that in France. The tax would
not be onerous, nor would it prevent any legitimate
advertiser from making use of that method of attract
ing the attention of the public to his wares if he chose,
but it would have the effect of putting a stop to the
display of many of those hideously ugly and enormous
placards that disgrace our cities.
There is no reason why those whomake use of walls
and fences along the public streets to get a profit
from them should not be made to pay a fair amount
of tax. The tax. too, might be made to settle the
question of. the height of permissible billboards. . If
a properly graded tax were imposed they would not
go too high. This is one of the things in which we
can learn something from Paris*. -
FRENCH POSTER TAXES.
Political economists and other philosophers have
been of the opinion that the drift of population frpm
the rural districts of England to the towns is due to
the decline of agriculture and the . higher rate of
wages in : . the* cities; but one laborer, when asked by
a commission tl;e other day why he had left the coun
try, replied: "I could not stand it, sir. There wasn't
an alehouse within a mile of the farm."
With such high-sounding words and lofty senti
ments did the astute Minister make his olea ae-ninsf.
Having thus laid down the proposition that Ameri
cans and Chinese are alike in their belief in popular
sovereignty, the orator went on to point out that
commerce is drawing them nearer together and that
it is to the interest of both to extend and multiply
their commercial relations. Then he hastened to his
conclusion: "Your invitation to me shows that your
patriotism is not of that kind which cannot see any
thing good in the people of other lands, and the
motto of which is, "Our country, right or wrong.'
That is only national selfishness which goes by the
name of patriotism. It is a relic of bygone days,
when there- was comparatively little intercourse be
tween the people of orte country and those of an
other, and when a man could be free to enjoy life
and liberty only in the land of his birth. All of that
is now completely changed. Happily we live in a
more humane and enlightened age. National inter
ests have gradually given way to world interests, and
the day is not far distant when love of country will
be secondary to humanity as a virtue. This: nation
has not sprung into existence without a manifest des
tiny. It was established * * * to treat all peo
ple alike with fairness and justice: to do away with
all selfish and clannish feelings; to make American
patriotism synonymous with fair play, with love of
mankind, with full freedom and liberty in accord with
law and justice."
The argument opens with comparison of the Gov
ernment of China with that of the United States, and
the claim is made that in China as in the United
States there is a complete recognition of popular
sovereignty. Minister Wu went on to say: "The
difference between the two countries lies in the man
ner in which the principle is carried out. You be
lieve in the choice of officers through the ballot-box;
we believe in the choice of public officers through the
examination hall. In this country all men are equal
at the polls. * * * The man who has grown gray
in the service of his country, as well as the man who
has just taken out his naturalization papers, has no
more than one vote. Every vote counts as much as
any other, and those who get the most. votes are en
titled to represent the people. *__* * In China we
endeavor to attain the same end by a different road.
The competitive examinations are open to all. All
candidates stand on the. same ground and have, an
equal chance of success. â¢ * * * In that way a cer
tain proportion of the ruling class of the land is
drawn directly from the people."
FULL reports of the Fourth of July address de
livered by Minister Wu Ting- fang at Philadel
phia^have now been received in our Eastern ex
changes, and judging by the comments of several of
the leading papers of that, section of the country it
was a distinguished success. A careful reading -of it
shows that from first to last it was a subtle'and clever
plea against any further extension of the Chinese ex
clusion act The Minister, of course, did not refer
to the act, but none the less the logic of his argument,
if accepted, would carry with it a condemnation of
any measure of the kind.
MINISTER WU'S ORATION.
rr.d appreciated. Our government in the Philippines
is purely autocratic, with no popular feature in it.
A similar community of Americans would destroy it
":i an hour rather than submit to it a minute. This
i<; hot denying that it is good government, superior
to any the Filipinos bsve known, but it is a despot
ism imposed from without. That three of its eight
members are natives does not change it, for they
were not elected by the people. That part of the
Fystern is copied from England's scheme in India,
where there is a minority of natives in the Council,
uot enough to make things go right, but enough to
be accused of responsibility if they go wrong.
It is necessary to say these things in order that our
cwn people may not deceive themselves as to the
problem in the islands. It is not solved by the pres
ent scheme of government, nor by the cessation of
armed resistance in rrany of the provinces. It will
not be solved by making Territories of the islands,
for that makes them potential States, and our people
will rot have them as States, and will grind to pow
der any party that proposes it. Let the truth be told
The financial report from the islands shows that
we have taken out of them the same taxes as Spain
did, $10,000,000 a year. So in denial of self-govern
ment and burden -of taxation they are just
where they were when Dr. Rizal and Aguin
aldo made their first stand for freedom in
1896. They cannot effectively reach the source of the
laws that govern them any more now than they could
â¢when Weyler was the Captain General. Those laws
could be just as well passed in San Francisco and ex
ported to them.
But that is neither self-government nor a step in
that direction, long or short. No one cares to dis
cuss the islands. The country seems tired of that.
But let us not confuse our own ideas of self-govern
ment If the eight men who are to legislate for
twelve millions of people had been elected, by those
people," then their power and the laws they pass
would be derived from the people affected by them.
If the laws were oppressive the oppressed could re
peal them by electing other legislators for that pur
pose. But the Philippine legislature is not elected by
the people, nor are they consulted in its appointment,
r.or are natives given a majority in its membership,
nor can the people displace it and elect others. The
plan is just as far away from self-government as was
the Spanish rule. It is, in form, exactly the same
despotism, the difference in our favor being its
greater benevolence.
THE Oakland Enquirer, a paper edited with
quite unusual care and discrimination, makes an.
odd blunder in its characterization of the new
Philippine government The five American members
of the commission, with three natives, constitute the
legislative department of the government They are
all appointed from Washington. The Enquirer says:
"It is a pretty long Â«tep in the direction of giving
the Filipinos self-government to make three island
ers members of the legislative body which frames
government for the provinces and municipalities,
makes appropriations, establishes schools and enacts
all other important laws."

It is possible that British politicians feel the need
of something to rouse the flagging jingo spirit at
home and to divert the rising sympathy in the world
for the forlorn Boers. Nothing could do 'this* more
effectually than such' a story. But suppose it is true,
still there is Kitchener and the record of Omdurman.
The public opinion of the world w ; U receive the
story of Boer outrage of the wounded with much
caution. The Dutch are a race accustdmed to* face
peril and go into hard undertakings. Their battles
have always been against a,, superior force. When
Spain was as strong as'^ Great Britain is now the
Dutch withstood her, under circumstances as forlorn
as those in South Africa, and finally won by sheer
persistence and courage. Yet in all that dreary and
bloody struggle for the independence of The Nether
lands there is no record of their slaughter of the
wounded or other violations of fair war. That was
more than two centuries ago, when the war code was
not as scrupulous as it is now and combatants were
permitted a.greater latitude. If 4hey refrained then,
when fighting the savage Alva, it is not likely that
they yield to temptation now. â¢' -
In a late delivery Lord Salisbury admitted that
England was hated, and to be safe in her empire must
also be feared. Her South African policy will cer
tainly not mitigate any hatred that others may feel
for her, though it may inspire that fear which seems
necessary to her^velfare.
IT is inconceivable that if the unsigned story of,
killing the wounded by the Boers were true it
would be censored by the British authorities.
It happens that the British commander in South
Africa is Lord Kitchener, whose wholesale slaughter
of the wounded at the battle of Omdurman appalled
the world, and was freely published. by the British
press. Under the circumstances it is hardly possible
that his Lordship would rigidly censor the news of
like conduct by the Boers, as his misery in that con
nection is of the kind that 'loves company. At the
same time it must be admitted that his- military policy
is calculated to provoke "desperate reprisals.. The
wholesale destruction of Boer homes and farmsteads,
atid the driving of women and children and aged non
corfibatants like cattle into stockades, where the little
ones perish by hundreds, is not calculated to pro
mote "a gentle frame of mind in the fathers and
brothys of these helpless victims of a ruthless policy.
The banishment of Cronje and the exiling of military
prisoners to the various hells which Great Britain
.owns in the tropics is another measure that prolongs
the war and increases its horrors.

It will be perceived that Wu's speech was highly
successful from his point of view. It is not strictly
true that he has revolutionized public sentiment, as
the Baltimore American says, but he has evidently
accomplished a good deal in that direction. Upon
the exclusion act, therefore, it behooves Californians
to prepare themselves for a hard fight. . Minister Wu
is not an opponent to b'e despised.
_LL.D., _was gracefully and forcibly "portraying in In
dependence square the high ethical principles that
cluster around Independence day, the. inveterate ene
mies of his race, even the California Congressmen,
were vaunting their design and ability to. prolong for
another twenty years after May next the peculiarly
despotic end un-American edict of exclusion now in
force in this country against the brilliant Dr. Wu's
the (exclusion act. The Baltimore American, in com
menting^pon it, said: "Minister Wu, .without craft,
has succeeded in revolutionizing public opinion in the
IJnited States with regard to his country." The Phil
adelphia Record' said: While ,'Wu Ting-fang,
All men know that no matter upon what pretext
or upon what profession of virtue the Democracy
achieve power, they will use it to again disorder the
revenue and financial system of the country, with the
same old result which has always followed such
erratic change in policy and unsettling of the foun
dations of prosperity. If Republican rings and bosses
repeat frequently the misdeeds which are a stench in
Pennsylvania, they will be responsible for a party
disaster which will be also a misfortune to the coun
try. The Call makes no excuse for its constant effort
to secure Republican practice of the virtue professed
by the Democracy, for in that, and that alone, lies
the future safety of the party.
' Had the Democracy not thought the Whig party
dead in 1838-39 the Whigs would not have carried
the election of 1840. When a large element, in a tri
umphant party feels that the opposition is dissolved,
only the sternest measures can restrain it from
prodigal use of its power.
The Whigs were out of power in 1839 and the Dem
ocrats were in. The defalcations of Federal officers
in New York' and the charges against, Van Buren's
administration were used to convince the reason of
the people that the party in power was not living up
to its obligation to practice virtue, and that failure,
contrasted with the Whig professions, revived a party
declared to be dead, and after its official epitaph had
been written by its enemy. Not only that, but in
1848 it performed a feat that stands singular in Amer
ican politics. Â¦ It expelled from power the Democ
racy whose administration had just successfully car
ried on a popular foreign war. These incidents are
admonitory, and the Republican party everywhere
must be admonished by them. Hard times produce
political changes, and so. do good times. All men do
not consider themselves the equal beneficiaries of
good times, and as the profits of enterprise increase
so do the grudges which they arouse. Add to this
outgrowth of human envy a reckless course of con
duct on the part of the party in power, and there are
present the. .elements of a political reyolution. Al
ready it is apparent that the profound minds in Re
publican leadership appreciate this situation, and
hence such a bold stroke against the leckless ma
chine as that made by Postmaster General Smith.
-This history is of interest in view of the courageous
stand taken by the Postmaster General against the
Republican machine in Philadelphia. We lay it down
as an axiom in American politics that the party out
of power needs only to profess virtue, while the party
in power must practice it.
Â¦Yet the Whig party svept the country the next
year by a popular majority of 146,315, and by 234
electoral votes to Democracy's 60!
IN October, 1839, the Democratic Review, long the
exponent of 'that : party, announced the death/ of
the -Whig party. The Review said: "Well; the
summer and autumn elections are now for^the most
part .over,' and what have the Whigs left to say -for
themselves and their exhausted and exploded 4 cause?
Will they, can they/ pretendto maintain* any longer
even a show of regular opposition? Will they, can
they, attempt seriously to contest the coming Presf
dentiarelection? We find it difficult to imagine "that
they will or can." . . ..

OBSERVATIONS ON THOSE WHO
PUT THEIR TRUST UN TRUSTS
RECENT reviews and * magazines
have contained many articles on.
the dangers of trusts and others on
the advantages of combinations of
capital. Notably in the May North
American Review are a number, all, with
tho exception of one by Russell Sage, rad
ically favorable to the system of combin
ing capital and industrial enterprises.
A consideration of these articles leads
to the conclusion that our thinkers who
are out of the trusts see little but pros
pects of evil from sucli unprecedented
and gigantic combinations, while the
other class, principally those in the trusts,
see no real dangers and only prosperity
and progress, and anticipate that the
world is through the trusts entering upon
its first real and endless prosperity.
(Faith is always beautiful, be it faith In
God, faith in man or faith in money. I
have observed, however, that one who can
put unlimited faith in money usually has
next to no faith in God and little in man.
. To read one side only of this controversy
a person would naturally be led to sup
pose that trusts could do no good In any
way to any one. By the other It is made
to appear that they will go on benefiting
mankind and blessing the world forever.
Obviously the questions raised and now
being discussed came â¢up naturally; they
are the result of inventions, modern Im
provements and methods of doing busi
ness. To say this, however, is not to say
that they are right or wrong. Weeds
grow more naturally than grain, and be
cause a course is the one being pursued
does not prove it right nor prove it wrong,
and it may be and it usually is true that
any course partly the result of circum
stances and partly the. result of design is
partiyright and partly wrong.
Faith, of Trust Makers.
It is not surprising that those who cre
ated the trusts and whose wealth thus far
It least has been almost immeasurably In
creased thereby should admire them and
have only good reports thus far to make
and favorable anticipations of the future.
Such has been the habit of creators and
ownors from' the beeinning.
It is written that when God created the
heavens and the earth and all was fin
ished . he looked upon his work and
said, "It is good"; but even an* all-wise
mind was, either observing as to immedi
ate appearances or did not comprehend
the capacity of his creatures to turn good
into evil.
The creators of the trust assume that
they, have been tried and found perfect.
As a matter of fact they have not been
tried at all. All the great modern indus
trial combinations, except the Standard
Oil and a few others, have been formed
during the present era of good times. No
dark clouds have as yet lowered over the
house'of the trusts, no stress of any finan
cial depression or great strike or indus
trial disaster, has occurred to test their
alleged stormproof character. Thus far
they have journeyed over favorable seas,
wafted by prosperous winds, comforted
with plenty to do and many to buy, large
salaries and larger dividends. Is It any
wonder that the trusts and their bene
ficiaries are as happy and as hopeful as
lovers on their honeymoon and apparently
have as little thought of squalls and
troubles to come? That hard times will
come no man can doubt, and when they
do come, with their attendant train of
troubles and disaster and bitter discon
tent, then and not until then will the
trusts be tested and their perpetuity de
cided. The organizers' of trusts are not
only optimistic to a degree, but exceed
ingly self-sufficient and intolerant of crit
icism or interference.
Trust Organizers Resent Criticism.
Those from the outside who study them,
question them, criticize them or threaten
in any manner to control them are at
once ostracised and execrated and classed
as "meddlesome newspapers," "agitat
ors," "politicians" (a particularly low
order of creation in the minds of these
modern solons with Croesus attach
ments), "demagogues," "charlatans,"
"mountebanks" and the like.
Jt Is not wise by offensive language to
be driven to resentments or to assume be
cause an advocate is intolerant and of
fensive that he is necessarily wrong in all
claims or conclusions. These gentlemen
who write so learnedly, even interestingly,
are all advocates of the doctrine that
work can only be well done by specialists
and, not being themselves specialists in
magazine work or in literary discussion,
have doubtless proven the correctness of
their own views by not making as good
or courteous presentation as might have
been made with the material at hand. A
tyro in discussion is always contemptuous
of his adversary and Intolerant of oppo
sition. One is constrained to the belief,
however, that in some instances these
very wise and wealthy gentlemen are au*
ihors in name only and have been dis
creet enough to employ specialists to do
their literary work.
- âââ, the fo'lowin- article but present, them for whatever va!u. they
CThe Ctll does not hold Itself responsible for the opinions published in the fo.low to* article, d
â¢r have as communications of general interest.) AmHkmnMi an fl Â»*,
bered, entitled to that respect that a*â¢?*
attaches itself to great wealth, a million
a year, not to mention dividends and
profits nor sucji little things as an unusual
amount of energy and much Practical
sense, all of which he possesses. But an
illustration may be drawn from his posi
tion and his argument. God and nature
filled tho earth with coal and iron. The
rivera, lakes and seas are highways sup
posed to be useful and beneficial to all
mankind. Railroads exercise the rlgnt or
eminent domain because they are. sup
posed to serve the public. But if the Fed
eral Steel Trust owns its own mines of
coal and Iron and they happen to be all
of the best and most available acquired
at nominal costs, and if they own their
own ships and railroads and factories and
fix the price of labor, the cost of produc
tion, tho expense of transportation ana
the price paid by consumers, and ail or
these questions are to be settled "in the
counting room and in the factory," and
without antagonistic action either upon
the part of the people or Congress, it
will beco"me true that government created
for the protection of all of the people must
abandon Its supposed sovereignty over all
of its subjects to a few of its subjects, or
is It the idea of our-friends'of the trusts
that governments are organized for police
purposes only to protect property and peo
ple that own property?
It Is assumed that the President of the
United States earns In his hitherto con
sidered great and responsible office fifty
thousand dollars per year enly and that
the Federal Steel Trust is dealing fairly
with consumers 'when it pays from their
contributions to its president one million
dollars per year, when all the people can
afford to pay their President such a com
paratively small salary. The Â¦whole situ
ation is preposterous, unnatural and Im
possible to be perpetuated and could only
be thought expedient or just by a Judge
sitting in his own case to decide for him
self and all others. It is true, we hope,
that broader, saner, fairer counsels than
these will prevail. It should not be as
sumed because we have great and power
ful combinations of capital we need Ies3
government and next to no government,
but upon the contrary we all need more
government, more intelligent government,
stronger government, more honest and
better government than ever before.
Control of Combinations. \
Money alone never did make mep Just
or wise. It is not written or observable
in history that great accumulators of
wealth were wise beyond their fellows or
qualified to judge better than others upon
questions pertaining to the science of gov
ernment or- what was likely to bring hap
piness or true greatness to a generation
or a race of men.
The counting house and the factory
have their uses and their places not In
conspicuous or secondary- Those in con
trol of them are, many of them, possessed
of brains, energy and good sense not to
be despised in counsel. But they show
neither wisdom nor good sense when they
assume to- possess the right or power to
ignore the just and necessary control of
government over all persons and corpora
tions. Pernaps they do not intend to as
sume such a position or to be understood
as doing so, but that they actually do so
assume. and pretend is "written In terms
in the articles referred to. â¢
Such an assumption of unrestrained
power in combinations of capital amounts
to monopolistic socialism confined to its
operators and stockholders. The assump
tion- of such a position and the execution
of the programme now under way can re
sult only in the tendering of an issue that
can ultimately be decided in one way only.
Rich men are ambitious. They want more
power, more wealth and more control.
But others are also ambitious and want
some wealth, some power and some con
trol.
If combinations of capital are beyond
control of governments as now conducted,
and they appear to be at least tending
that way, the inevitable tendency of the
opposition will be toward like combina
tion through public ownership and public
operation, amounting Â»re " long to state
socialism, in which many broad and hon
est men see many dangers to our form
of government, our civilization and our
race. But government must and will b<;
government and ultimately must control.
The question is, shall it control in its
present form or in a form and manner
much less satisfactory to capital and
vested Interests?
Those who love our institutions as they
have been and are (at least in a degree
they are still the same) see little to hope
for in such exhibitions of. superlative con
ceit as the organizers and those in con
trol of the trusts are now furnishing.
Should Not Seek Industrial Control.
The assumption that all government
control is meddling; that all officials who
do not defer without question to the lords
of the "counting house and the factory"
are mischievous meddlers. That all news-
all politicians are demagogues and that
all statesmen are dead, 13 not likely to
settle wisely or well these or any other
problems. Our only hope is that a saner
time and a soberer view will come before
the inevitable other extreme 13 upon us
and our Institutions are Irrevocably anl
injuriously changed. Equality, character
and good government, not monopoly or
socialism, have accumulated the wealth
we enjoy and achieved the position wa
now occupy as a nation and a people.
Suppose we succeed as suggested in be
in" by the trusts "put in indus
trial control of the world," which
is about as sane an Idea as
Monte Cristo's exclamation, "the world
is mine." What would we do with
the industrial control of the world it -wa
had It? "We could remain in control only
by competition with the toil and poverty
and comparatively helplessness of the
world's labor. We may begin such com
petition in pride and conceit but it can
only end In disaster. It can be continued
for a time and for a time only by use of
our superior machinery and our more in
telligent labor, but to assume that other
countries cannot even Imitate us or uso
the same machinery or- copy the ways of
our labor (some of which has been but
recently imported) is too much conceit for
practical business uses.
Trust God and the Constitution.
What we need is to be "put in con
trol" of ourselves and our own govern
ment, protect our own markets, develop
our own resources and protect our own
people from outsiders <nd from each
other- and preserve all that 13 best in our
form of government, laws and civilization
and institutions.
Suppose other countries organize trusts
of their own, we cannot claim to have
copyrighted these peculiarly wise and
beneficent organizations that supply the
place of government, remove competition,
and all other ills and woes at will. So If wo
cease to, have a monopoly in trusts our
commercial supremacy might ba called
seriously in question. Possibly, therefore.
It might be well to continue to be- so 014
fashioned as to put some of our trust in
Almighty Gcd. some in our constitution
and laws and some in our worth and In
tegrity, and not all of it in mere mone
tary wealth in the hands of millionaires
combined under corporations called trnst3.
The matter I should like to impress is
one dear to every thinking American: and
that is that the arrogance and" exactions
cf combined capital are rot likely to do
nearly so much harm in the temporary
exactions it m.n.r extort and obtain as the
inevitable tendency It generates to drive
the masses of the people to the other ex
treme, which other extreme, will be dis
astrous to capital, which would amount
to a temporary calamity only. The- far
graver Injury, however. Is that It will also
work a radical change in our form of gov
ernment and methods of life altogether
injurious and wholly unnecessary unless
the arrosance of organized wealth shall
render it possible and acceptable to the
people as their last choice between two
evils. FRANK IL SHORT.
