Borrowing wisdom from Intolerance it
may very truthfully be conceded that
combinations of cap.tal are in a position
to do much of the good claimed and may
do some part of it, and also that they are
likely to do much less good than they
prophesy and not all the harm anticipat
ed by some radical thinkers. It is true
that steam engines, steamships and steam
railroads,^ telegraphs, telephones, electric
transportation and their thousand kin
dred appliances afford opportunities for
beneficial industrial * co-operation and
combination such as never before existed.
That thereby much good can and ought
to be done. That the greater the system
the more perfect should be its operation.
The more direct the course from the raw
material to the hungry consumer the bet
ter the results ought to be and the lower
the price should be. As cited where the
steel trust owns its own mines of coal and
iron, its own ships, its own factories and
railroads it ought to. be able to produce
the best article possible for the least pos
sible price. That It does produce a good
article in a skilled and economical way is
undoubtedly true. That it sells as cheap
as possible to the consumer is not true.
High Salaries and Big Dividends.
That It sells at even a'reasonable profit
can hardly be true, as it seems to be ad
mitted that it sells certain of its products
in foreign markets . much cheaper than in
its own. Its salaries are ridiculously high
and its dividends on actual investments
are exorbitantly large. Mr. Schwab, for
instance. Is "paid $1,000,000 a .year for su
pervising, and is delighted with combina
tions of capital because -it reduces "the
item" of supervision, but since his salary
would pay that of 100 men at $10,000 each
per year, the steel trust seems to have
been more efficient in reducing numbers
than cash. Mr. .Schwab, obviously. not a
foolish man, would hardly get a million
a year as a logician. Mr. Schwab being
a supervisor magnifies the importance of
supervision. Being a* combine, he mag
nifies the advantages and beauties of
combinations. Being in a great trust,; he
magnifies, the benefits of trusts and min
imizes the importance of the functions
of government; in. fact, unconsciously, no
doubt, he infers that government is a
mere side issue that.will do no harm if it
keeps "hands off" of '' trusts. He (Mr.
Schwab) thinks the whole question will
be allowed to work itself out "in the fac
tory", and in the "counting .room." . "The
Iron and steel industry of America is not
apprehensive of antagonistic political ac
tion toward it, either by the people or by
Congress.' 1 Antagonistic interference is
evidently v referred to as anything pre
venting the full control of all questions
involved "in : the factory and counting
room." . ' SSSflfeP^^&gSgjSJfcg
Mr. Schwab's Million.
Mr. SchTvab Is, be it always - renjem-

EPWORTH LEAGUE COMMITTEES
GREET VISITING DELEGATES
The Registrar's office is open every
evening from 7:30 to 9:30 for purposes of
registration and recording changes of res
idence since November 6, 1900. Election
officers are also being sworn in at a rapid
rate and all arrangements for tire primary
election on August 13 are nearlng comple
tion.
Registrar's Office Opsn Evenings.
A significant commentary upon the
state of affairs that h3s arisen at the
Mint, whether due to Treasury Â¦ Depart
ment official orders or to lack of pre
caution on the part of Superintendent
Leach is afforded by the fact that $47,000,
000 in gold is now stored in the basement
vaults, where previously only silver was
stored. they not being deemed safe
enough for the more precious coin. Upon
the. discovery of the theft this amount of
gold coin In sacks was transferred from
the two cashier's vaults to the basement
vaults, where it is being carefully guard
ed by an extra force of watchmen while
inspection of the treasure proceeds.
Responsibility for the $30,000 stolen will
be difficult to fix. The Treasury Depart
ment will no doubt contend that Superin
tendent Leach's bond must make It good,
and the Superintendent will pass the re
sponsibility along to Cashier Cole. The
two local officials will probably join in
the contention that the fault lies with
the -Treasury Department officials, whose
orders are responsible for the plethora of
coined gold that made the theft of the
$30,000 possible. , â¢'
The inspection of the treasure in the big
vaults went on yesterday from 9 o'clock
In the morning till .4 In the afternoon.
Nearly $4,000,000 was counted and not a
single sack found to be shy of its proper
amount of coin. This inspection will re
quire about ten days yet for its comple
tion. It is not thought that further
thefts v/ill be discovered.
Treasure in Basement Vaults.
The private life of the suspected man Is
being scanned as closely as his conduct
at the Mint. Both phases contain damag
ing evidence, but not the clean-cut sort
that convicts.' Dlmmlck was surprised In
the act, practically, of imitating the sig
nature of Superintendent Leach. He is
known to have taken money from the pay
envelopes of employes, making restitution
at later dates. He made remarks to
Cashier Cole that seemed to be "feelers"
put out cautiously In search of a confed
erate in a . plot to loot the vaults. A
mass of evidence has been uncovered
that points the finger of suspicion at Dlm
mick. But apparently that Is all. No one
saw him taking treasure from the vault;
none of It has been found in his posses
sion, and no sack' or tag or other bit of
evidence has been found to give a claw
to the disposition made of the loot. Fel
low employes of Dimmlck predict, while
assuming his guilt, that he will never be
convicted.
Director Roberts, Superintendent Leach,
Secret Service Agent Hazen, Cashier
Cole and Chief Clerk Dimmlck were
closeted together during most of the day's
investigations. Charles Waltz, the lock
expert, was present during several hours
of the afternoon and it is believed that his
reports on the combinations of the locks
to the cashier's working vault were dam
aging to Dimmlck.' Waltz was employed
to determine whether or not Cashier Cole
changed the combination to a degree that
the chief clerk, who previously held the
combination, .would not be able to open
the vault doors. .
Experiments by Lock Expert.
Dlmmlck jj remains at liberty, but the
surveillance of secret service men is not
relaxed for a moment and flight, It is al
leged, would be impossible for him. In
justice to him it must be said that he has
shown no disposition to shrink from the
investigation, but those aniong the Mint
employes who are convinced of his guilt
say his attitude la due to confidence that
convicting evidence against him cannot be
obtained. .
The longest session yet held of Mint of
ficials and secret service men was that
of yesterday. Beginning at 10 o'clock, It
lasted, with but an hour for lunch at
noon, till almost 7 o'clock in the evening.
When It was over 'Chief Clerk Dlmmlck
bore the look of a man who had been un
der fire and who realized that he had not
came out of it with credit. On the
other hand. Director of the Mint Roberts,
Superintendent Leach and Cashier Cole
evidently felt pleased and almost elated
over the results of the day's investigation.
"While the men who are probing into the
mystery are keeping their own counsel,
there are numerous Indications that the
meshes of the law are tightening about
the chief clerk and that the Investigators
no longer fear that the thief, whoever he
may be, will escape detection.
the Mint.

rested before night on the charge of hav
ing stolen the $30,000 in gold that la miss
ingr from the cashier's working vault of

a LTHOUGH almost a \reek must
i\ yet intervene before the Epworth
/\\ Leagxie Convention will com-
A. j\ mence Its sessions, the head
quarters at Mechanics' Pavilion
have already assumed an air of intense
activity. To accommodate the hundreds
of delegates who have already arrived, the
registration and entertainment commit
tees remained at their poets all day yes
terday. It was the intention of the local
leaguers to have sent representatives to
receive the delegates when they stepped
from the overland trains, but their early
arrival disrupted all the plans. Imme
diately after registering yesterday the
major part cf the visitors left on excur
sion trains to visit points of interest in
various parts of the State. They will not
return until the opening of the conven
tion
The registration and entertainment
committees have already established
themselves at Mechanics' Pavilion and
their example will be followed to-day by
aJl the rest of the executive force.
The work of transforming the interior
cf the Pavilion into a magnificent audi
torium is steadily progressing. The
framework for the placing- of from 8000 to
8000 chairs is about completed and it is
expected that the seats will all be in po
sition to-day.
It has been stated by the railroad au
thorities as a conservative estimate that
there are now 23.000 excursionists on their
way to this city and that as many more
are expected.
Deppjte the great' benefit that the Ep
worth Leagu- convention.* with its thou-
Fands,of visitors, will confer upon this
city and Etate. the subscriptions are still
coming in very slowly. The managers of
the convention need nothing now to insure
\
The New York special train, which Is
scheduled to arrive at Denver this morn-
Ing, is bearing more than 150 delegates.
On Saturday and Monday the heaviest
traffic on the Ogden route is expected.
There will be at least twenty excursion
trains and in the neighborhood of fifty
extra carp.
It is estimated by the railroad compa
nies that they have already transported
700 delegates to the city and that within
the next two days the number will be
swelled to 5000. The main body of dele
gates however, will not arrive until Mon
day or Tuesday. From all over the Union
come reports each day of Increases in the
army which is on the way. The local
agents of the railroad companies have
been informed that immense numbers of
visitors are coming from New England
and the Middle and Southern States.
Word was received that 400 delegates and
their friends departed on a special train
bearing the frame Golden Gate from Phil
adelphia yesterday morning. There are
four other special trains speeding from
Philadelphia. carrying at least 500
leaguers. They will leave Denver this
morning and arrive in Ogden early to
morrow.
Delegates on the Way.
The representation by the Humboldt
farmers of their giant trees is about com
plete. So perfect has the resemblance
been made that it is almost impossible
to distinguish the imitation from the
original.
The Stanford University booth, decked
In a brilliant hue of red, will contain an
exhibit of the results of the State's edu
cational advancement.
Across the aisle leading to the booths
will be placed an arch of welcome, deco
rated lavishly with bunting, various
colored streamers and a wealth of beauti
ful blossoms and foliage. During the
night this arch will be brilliantly illumi
nated with electric lights. ; Â¦
In the line of displays the two principal
transcontinental railroad companies are
both striving after the picturesque and
unique. The Santa Fe will have as its
headquarters a booth representing one of
the old California missions, with its roof
of ti'.es and its walls of old gray stone.
As a historical representation this dis
play will be one of the features of the
convention.
The Southern Pacific Company will en
deavor to display the products, resources
and picturesque scenery of all California.
This display is being devised by Wiiliam
Murray of the company's information bu
reau. "When it is completed it will be un
der the charge of E. K. Gordon.
Giant Trees Reproduced.

noon. The singing of the grand chorus,
which is the largest ever assembled on
the coast, accompanied by the magnificent
Stanford organ, will be one of the most
memorable features of the Epworth
League Convention.
The section of the gallery which has
been devoted to the booths is rapidly be
ing: converted Into a veritable maze of
streaming bunting. Almost all the coun
ties of the State are preparing to exhibit
to the Easterners their produce and each
is striving to outdo the other. Santa
Clara County Intends to make a specialty
of her prune industry. Besides construct
ing an imposing pyramid of her luscious
fruit, that county proposes to feed the
delegates on prunes.
its success so -much as financial support.
In' the course of a few days the great host
of delegates will be here. They will leave
thousands of do'lars in circulation in this
city, and it is incumbent upon the citi
zens to contribute to their entertainment.

A New Departure in
San Francisco Shoe Selling
The world-famed Regal Shoes are now on sale in this city.
The Store at the corner of Geary and Stockton Streets is the only
one West of the Missouri River where shoes are sold direct from tannery
to consumer.
This is. the Regal method. The manufacturers of Regal Shoes own
and maintain retail stores in all the principal cities of the country. The
San -Francisco store is the latest to be established.
Shoe's sold by ordinary methods are handled many times unnecessarily.
This handling costs money. It makes a shoe of the same value as the Regal
cost more. It makes a shoe sold at the Regal price worth much less. The
Regal method eliminates this handling. It saves to the shoe wearer all these
unnecessary profits. It .gives to the shoe wearer $3.50 worth of real
shoe value for $3.50. It gives a shoe the equal in every respect of any $6.00
shoe sold by ordinary methods.
All styles of Regal Shoes sell at the one price^ â $3*50. The styles
are exact duplicates of the patterns now made by the high priced custom
bootmakers of London, Paris and New York.
Regals are made in 162 styles and 121 sizes â a style for every taste
âa fit for every natural foot.
There are 26 styles of Oxfords, (low cut) the popular Summer shape.

CHICAGO. " July 10.â Influenced by strength
in coarse Grains, wheat opened firm, and on
light offers the market continued to rule firm
throughout the day. Trade was. moderate, the
crowd continuing to favor corn . and oats, as
promising better results to buyers tinder ex
iatir.s hot weather conditions. Demand was
mostly by shorts, while commission houses
were the sellers. September opened %c to %c
higher at 64VÂ»c to 64%c, and advanced stead
ily, closing %c higher at 64%c, the high point
of the day.,
Exceedingly hot temperatures over Â»a large
section of the country created excitement in
tho corn pit at the opening. There was an
enormous demand both local and on outside
account early, and it was hard to execute
orders, as scarcely any corn was on the mar
ket, and what was for sale was held by thosÂ»
waiting to take profits. Heavy realizing on
the â¢ advance caused a sharn decline, but en
renewed buying pries rallied again. Septem
ber corn closed %@%c higher at 49%c.
There was about" as bier a market for oats
as for corn. Weather conditions were the bull
influences, together with sympathy with corn.
September closed %@%c higher at 31%c.
Provisions opened steady with a firm under
tone. The damare to corn and oats by heat
and the drying up of pastures had a, stimulat
ing effect. . September pork closed SOc higher,
lard 13c higher and ribs liic higher.

was very generally affected, the Eastern trunk
lines sharing in the fullest of the day's weak
ness. ' Indications show that speculative buyers
of the recent past and even Ions time holders
who refused to be shaken out by the May
panic were among the sellers. The growing
conviction that there would be no sufficient
ease of money before the fall demand for. mov
ing the crops to Invite active speculation was
borne in upon speculators who were holding
large lots in anticipation of a midsummer buy
ing movement, and they threw over stocks in
large volume under the conviction that money
conditions might .be worse before they are
better again. - ,
The gold export was the most important fae
tor in causing this feeling. The fact that in
terior hanks are already feeling the demand
for money from the agricultural centers is an
indication that that demand on New York will
come unusually early this year. A possible
source of relief was by imports of gold from
abroad and foreign money markets have cal
culated upon supplying gold for such a move
ment. An outward movement of gold after the
July settlements abroad and when exchange
bills against future payments of grain are al
ready in large supply in the New York mar
ket comes as a shock. Probably the unsettled
financial conditions in Germany explain the
movement, which must be a special one, al
though the plans of the Austro-Hungarian Gov
ernment to attract gold for currency purposes
may have its part.
The announcement of the engagement found
the market well above last night on a con
tinuation of yesterday's recovery, but liquida
tion set in at once and was continued in in
creasing volume until the close, which was
active and* weak at about the lowest The net
declines in the market leaders reached such fig
urs as the following: Rock Island 8, St. Paul
a%, Atchison 5%. Union Pacific o'i, Missouri
Pacific b%, Kansas and Texas preferred 1%,
Louisville and Nashville 4%. Iowa Central 5,
do preferred 7% and Chicago and Eastern Illi
nois 6. .
In other prominent active stocks, including
some of the Eastern trunk lines and leading
specialties, prices are between 2% and 4 points
lower qn the day. The steel stocks are about
two points lower.
The unbroken drouth in the corn belt and an
effort to discount an expected favorable show
ing of the Government's monthly crop report
were elements in the weakness of the market,
but were not sufficient to account for the gen
eral weakness Fhown.
The railroad bond market was -fairly active
at lower prices. Total sales, $3,095,000.
United States refunding 2s declined 14 per
cent on the last call.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Exchange and silver unchanged.
JVhcat weak and quiet. Barley showing some improvement.
Oats and Corn still excited in the West.
Com higher here and red Oats lozver.
Hav vscakcr under larger arrivals. Fecdstuffs firm.
Beans nozv wanted for shipment.
Potatoes weakening again. Tomatoes in heavy supply.
Butter advancing sharply on the Exchange.

Poultry market lightly supplied and firm.
Leading fresh fruits in demand and firm.
Fresh supplies of Bananas in from the islands.
Xcw evaporated Apples in demand and rising.
New dried Apricots and Peaches bringing good prices.
Provisions quoted as before.

improved business in local stocks and bonds.

New York Money Market.
LONDON. July 10. -Atchison, 84%; Canadian
Pacific, 105U : Anaconda. 9% ; Northern Pacific
preferred, 96 l ,i; Union Pacific' preferred, â¢ 92V4.

The stock market to-day was much more
cheerful than yesterday, but it was rather a
reaction from weakness than anything really
specific In the way of improvement. Business
was only moderate, eave In the American de
partment, which was more active than lately.
The strength and buoyancy exhibited in New
Y.ork yesterday decidedly demoralized the local
bears and to-day there was a 'fresh crop of
bulls, particularly in Atchison. The Continent
was a slight seller only. New York's buying
gave an early boost to Louisville, which was
talked of as going on a 6 per cent basis. On
the official opening Atchison was sold heavily,
causing a flat finish, Louisville and Nashville
alona maintaining strength. The weakness was
partly due to talk about a Western heat wave.
Money ruled steady. â¢

Â£2, to Â£115 5s. This great break- in prices for

at New York was marked down 75 points with
the closing price standing at $27 25Â§27 75, and
ihe tone was weak at the close.

Nczv York Metal. Market.
LONDON, July 10.â The offerings at the w^ol
auction sales to-day numbered 1173 bales.
American buyers purchased a' fair -share of su
perior Merinos, including New South Wales,

land Islands and Punta Arenas were In fair

London Wool Sales.
The sales since January 1 amount to 114,852,600
pounds, against 70,272,600 pounds for the corre
sponding time last year.
The sales for the week in Boston amounted
to 3.670,500 pounds domestic and 212,200 pounds
foreign, making a total of 3,882.700 pounds,
against a total of 6.195,000 for ihe previous week
and a total of 1,316,000 pounds for the corre
sponding week last year.
Another fact which has also tended to im
part strength to the market Is the continued
firmness in the Territories. Quite a lot of de
sirable medium stock is reported to have sold
at 14c. Indications are that wools will be
cleaned up as thoroughly in Montana as they
were In Idaho, and as they have been in most
of the Territories this season.
The wool market has been less active the
past week, the intervention of a holiay, com
bined with the dull and unfavorable opening at
the London markets, naturally tending to re
strict. Reports received since the opening of
the London sales indicate some improvement.
The trade is feeling better than at the begin
ning of the week under review and this feel
ing has been greatly strengthened by the tenor
of the reports from the goods market.

Deciduous and Citrus Fruits.
The firmness in tree, fruits continues and the
market cleans up well. The dryers are now
buying Apricots, which has advanced the price
of small fruit $5 . per ton. Growers are not
contracting 'much with the canners, saying
that the latters' terms are too severe. Peaches
Pears and Plums are very firm. Figs and Nec
tarines are getting more plentiful. Melons
keep up well under light receipts, but Grapes
are .-weakening.
A fresh lot of Bananas came in from the
Islands yesterday. No price has yet bsen
fixed. Limes are still off the market.

CORNâ Hot weather is still reported in the
Western Corn belt; with no rain in sight. Ad
vices to Bolton, De Ruyter & Co. say that the
country throughout the West continues to buy
Corn on a large scale. The "Washington au
thorities say that Corn has been seriously dam
aged in Kansas. Missouri. Texas and Okla
homa, but that Nebraska and Iowa have thus
far escaped. But since the above statement
was issued Iowa reports a temperature of 100
to 105 degrees, with no rain, and says that the
crop | looks worse every day. Phillips .wires
from Kansas that the crop there will turn out
a big half yield. Snow says that the condition
of the whole, crop is 85.4 per cent, against an
average of 87; which indicates a crop of 2,140,
000,000 bushels." â¢
. The Chicago market eased off under enor

NEW RAILROAD OFFICERS
ASSUME THEIR DUTIES
ntzhugh and Herbert Get Into Har
ness and Settle Down to
Hard Work.
The two new appointees of the South
ern Pacific Company took charge of thÂ«ir
offices yesterday, and spent most of the
business hours receiving callers and get
ting their work into shape.
E. H. Fitzhugh has taken the place
formerly occupied by H. E. Huntingdon.
He said he was not long enough in the
office to make any definite statement of
his plarts yesterday, nor would he defi
nitely define the limits of his duties and
authority. That he will soon make him
self felt in the company is certain, for
from all appearances he will be the pro
noun of the president and will act in his
name In a great many ways that call for
high authority and discretion.
J. M. Herbert installed himself In Mr.
Flllmore's rooms yesterday morning and
immediately dug into the routine of the
office. He will spend a few days in get
ting himself into the swing of the busi
ness, and then will commence the sys
tematic touring of the divisions, nor will
he stop until he has traveled over every
bit of track the company possesses. He
will start on this tour probably next Mon
day, and will take it in sections, return
ing to San Francisco at the end of the
week, whenever possible.
Mr. Herbert said â¢ yesterday that he
found the system and methods of his new
office practically similar to the routines
of other roads, and as he has served as
manager of several railroad properties he
finds himself fitting into the new placo
easily and without delay.
DYEB RENAULT TAKES
DOSE OF CARBOLIC ACID
Suffering- From an Incurable Disease,
He Puts an End to His
Existence, i ,
Louis J. Renault, a dyer, doing business
at 330% Tenth street, committed suicide
yesterday by taking carbolic acid.
[ He was found dead In his room by his
! daughter, Mathilde. Renault was 61
! years old and a native of France. He
had been suffering for c long time with
valvular disease of the heart and lately
had ben very despondent and erratic In
his actions.
Evidence at the Inquest yesterday
showed that Renault had been practical
ly insane and irresponsible for several
months past.
The jury brought In a verdict that he
had Â¦ committed suicide while temporarily
Insane. , . Â¦ , , -
At 10 o'clock an alarm was sent Jn from
the same box, 39, and the firemen had to
renew their work. It appears that a piece
of oakum lodged in the rafters and a
spark from the original fire ignited the
inflammable stuff. The chemical engine
extinguished the blaze in quick time. The
damage to the building and contents is
said to amount to about $3000.
A fire broke out in the building .used as
a storehouse by the Pacific Refining and
Roofinf Company and the Guadalupe As
phalt Company, at Sixteenth and Missis
sippi -streets, last night and caused a loss
of $1000. A second alarm was sounded, but
the department got the fire under control
before it had gained much headway. A
nearby shed, in Â¦Â¦which was stored a large,
amount of pitch and other inflammable
material, was paved by the prompt action
of the firemen. â¢ Â¦
Spontaneous combustion caused a lively
blaze in the shipwright shop of George G.
Rundle at 209 Steuart street at 5 o'clock
last night. The oakum and tarred ropes
stored in the place, made excellent fuel
for the fire and the department had a
hard task subduing the flames. The
building was badly burned and the loss to
Mr. Rundle te said to have been con
siderable. C. E. Boudrou. who kept a
junk shop in the same building, suffered
slight damage. The families of Mrs;
Teamer and Mrs. Williams, who occupied
the upstairs portion of the house, suffered
considerable loss.

Sis Home.
Cornelius Jurey. formerly a member of
Company I, First California Volunteers,
died at his home, 1508 Bryant avenue,
Tuesday. He will be buried in Calvary
Cemetery to-morrow morning.
Jurey was but 23 years of age and was
a most popular young man. He served
with Captain Richter's company during
all the engagements participated in by the
California regiment. He -was presented
with a testimonial and medal by the
colonel of the First for conspicuous gal
lantry at San Pedro Macati. In the face
of a heavy fire he crossed a field and re
covered a great deal of ammunition which
his regiment was compelled to leave be
hind.

CHICAGO, July 10.â John A. Hin3ey,
former president of the board of control
of the endowment rank of the Knights cf
Pythias, appeare'd before the Supreme
Lodge to-day and admitted the report
submitted yesterday, to be. true." The re
port shows that the affairs of the endow
ment rank under Hinsey's management
drifted into a practical insolvency. Of
$500,000 invested under Hinsey's direction,
nearly two-thirds went into enterprises
which rendered them unavailable to pay,
death claims. Other funds went into
channels for which there, is no account
ing.
The former president of the Board of
Control declared that he had done his
best to keep the treasury of the endow
ment rank in a sound condition, but the
death Â¦ claims had mounted up, invest
ments had turned out failures and it had
been necessary to overdraw the rank's
account at the bank..' , ' .
The report which was compiled by the
present Board of Control, excepting Hin
sey and the Insurance Commissioners of
Illinois and Connecticut, does not charge
Hlnsey and others with Â» misusing the
funds, but simply states that they were
misused. To reporters Hinsey. said: .

LUNATIC TRIES
TO KIDNAP GIRL
Twice Invades a Young
Lady's Home in
Redlands.
Foiled in His Undertaking
by Her Mother's Presence
of Mind.
Special Dispatch to The Can.
SAN BERNARDINO, July 10.-N. TT.
Lamb, 30 years of age, occupies a padded
ceWat the County Hospital, where he was
placed this morning pending an examina
tion" by Superior Judge Campbell on an
insanity charge. Lamb created a sen
sation yesterday afternoon at Redlands,
where he suddenly became a raving
maniac. His misfortune is due, it is. said,
to the excessive use of cigarettes.
Â¦ Lamb at 4 o'clock yesterday entered
the home of Mrs. Sarah Watson, 3OSV3
Orange street, and informed .that lady
that for some time he had been in love
with her pretty 19-year-old daughter,
Miss Mabel .Watson, and was now ready
to take her to Boston, where she would
be provided for In a luxurious manner.
The madman was determined in his
Â¦wishes and made a thorough search of the
premises. Mrs. Watson was thoroughly
frightened, but sÂ«ceede\ In hiding Misa
Watson in the dining-room. Failing to
find her, Lamb left the place.
The maniac returned an hour later,
more determined than ever to take Miss
Watson. This time, by a clever ruse,
Mrs. Watson got the stranger out on the
street and. hÂ© disappeared. The police
were notified and after hunting several
hours for Lamb they abandoned further
search.
At 11 o'clock last night Lamb appeared
on a bicycle at a cigar store. He imme
diately took possession, and, after filling
his pockets with cigars and cigarettes,
opened several boxes and passed them
among the bystanders. Owing to his
threatening, attitude no one ventured to
arrest him. His next escapade was at
the Casa Loma Hotel, where he tried to
abduct a waitress. At midnight the
police succeeded in placing him in jail.
Lamb is a carpenter and has been a
resident of Redlands but three months.
No one knows whence he came.

There was a very quiet little wedding
at the cathedral yesterday at noon, when
Miss Isabelle Louise McDonald and Frank
S. Drady were married. The" B-ev. Father
C. E. Kennedy of St. Paul's officiated.
Only the immediate relatives of the con
tracting parties 'were present, no invita
tions having been issued for the ceremony
owing to the recent death of the bride's
brother. â¢ /'-\ .
Miss May Conway of Los Angeles at
tended the bride as maid of honor and
Will Moore was best man. The bride wora
a traveling gown of gray broadcloth, -with,
hat to match. After the ceremony Mr.
and Mrs. Drady left immediately for th<5
south, where they will spend the honey
moon.
Mr. Drady is well known and popular.
He is second lieutenant o{ the Eighth
California Volunteers and captain of com
pany A. League of the Cross Cadets. Hi3
bride is the daughter of P. McDonald.
The bride wore an elegant gown of Ivory
white satin elaborately trimmed with lace.
A veil and orange blossoms lent the fin
ishing touches to the elegant costume.
Miss Bowman, the bridesmaid, wore a
dainty gown of white organdie over whit*
silk. '
The Rev. Bradford Leavltt of the First
Unitarian Church was the officiating
clergyman. About 150 Invited guests wit
nessed the ceremony. The home was at
tractively decorated for the happy occa
sion, pink and green being the prevailing
colors. . -.- Â¦-'.Â¦-.

PERSONAL.
New Salmon Hatchery.
TEHAMA, July 10,-The Government
has established a salmon hatchery about
two miles east of here on Mill Creek, and
the necessary buildings are now being
erected.
PURE FOOD ACT
A DEAD LETTER
Former Senator Withing
ton Says It Is Now .
Worthless.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN DIEGO. July 10.â The. Union to
morrow, will print an interview with D. L.
Withlngton, former State Senator from
this district; who, in the 1895 term of the
State Legislature, fathered the bill which
is and has been known as the pure food
bill and which the Senator â¢declares was
knocked out by the adoption of the report
of the Code Commissionersâ A. C. Free
man, W. C. Van . Fleet and George J.
Denis â which has been incorporated in the
laws of 1901 and which became of effect
and in force the first of this month.
The pure food law approved by Gover
nor Budd on March 2S. provides In its first
section: "No person shall, within this
State, manufacture for sale", offer for sale
or sell any drug or article of food which
is adulterated within the meaning of this
act." Another and separate section of the
law fixes punishment, together with
added penalties. There was, moreover, a
provision in the old law that the provis
ions of the act should not apply "to mix
tures or compounds recognized as ordi
nary articles or ingredients of articles of
food, if each and every package sold or
offered for sale is distinctly labeled as
.mixtures or compounds, with the name
and per cent of each ingredient therein,
and are not injurious to health."
The new law of the Code Commission,
according to the Senator, Is so carefully
hedged around with phrases which almost
preclude a conviction under it, that there
is no chance, of ever getting a conviction.
The new law Is section 3S3 of the code
and provides that "every person who
knowingly sells, or keeps, or offers for
sale (the manufacturing is entirely left
out) or otherwise disposes of any article
of food, drink, drug or medicine, knowing
(the first "knowingly" seemed not enough
to protect the breaker of the law) that the
same is adulterated or has been tainted,
decayed or otherwise unwholesome or unfit
to-be eaten or druntf, with intent to per
mit the same to be eaten or drunk, is
guilty, etc."
"The commissioners," says the father,
of the bill, "seem not satisfied with pro
tecting the man who breaks the law once,
but they, have to do it three or four
times over. The District Attorney has
to prove knowledge on the part of the
offender twice, but he must prove an in
tent also. He must prove that the fellow
knows he' has it, that he knows that it is
adulterated and that he intends that \t
shall : 'be eaten, three strong protections
for*tlie offender. Not only that, but the
section; .providing that the adulterated
food,s which are not Injurious shall be
labeled is entirely stricken from the law
and the act of 1S95 has been so disfigured
that it is of no effect whatever. A con
viction under it is utterly impossible, and
that which the people of the Bast would
have been willing to pay at least $50,000 to
have done has been done by the Code
Commissioners without any one who was
interested in the first measure knowing
it."
W. C. Miller Attempts to Strangle
INSANE PRISONER TEIES
TO KILL HIS CELLMATE
William Bauer in the City-
Prison.
W. C. Miller, who is under sentence of
six months for carrying a concealed
weapon, became Insane in the" City Prison
last night and started to strangle William
Bauer, his cellmate. The latter's screams
attracted â¢ the attention of Corporal
Heggerty and Turnkeys Mahoney
and Murphy. Rushing to the cell they
seized Miller Just in time to prevent him
from making Bauer a subject for the
Coroner. As it was evident that the fel
low is deranged he was sent to the Re
ceiving Hospital and locked up in the de
tention ward. . . i
FENCING OF THE BANGE â¢
THE IMPORTANT QUESTION
DENVER, July 10.â The most Important
business transacted by the board of di
rectors of the American Cattle Growers'
Association at its meeting in this city,
which ended to-day, was to pass a reso
lution recommending that the next con
vention . eliminate f roin the constitution
the assessment feature of raiding money
to , defray extraordinary expenses of the
association, which has proved unpopular.
â¢ The board is working to preserve a
status quo on. the range fencing question
until. the next Congress meets and it is
seen ,what that body will do on the leas
ing question. . President Lusk will appoint
a committee to draft a leasing bill. The
board will meet in this city again on the
first -Monday in November., . ; â¢Â¦Â¦-.- .
! The estimates made by the Board of
Public Works for improvements to the
! Fremont and Buena Vista School build
! ings subjected that body to criticism at a
meeting of the Board of Education yesr
terday. The first named board sent in a
communication that the lowest bid for the
: Fremont- School was $12,755, for the'Fre^
mont Primary $1340 and for the Buena
Vista $15,2?5. These figures were consid
erably above the estimates made by the
J Board of Works' engineers and the bids
were recommended for rejection. Since
they are not within the estimates, the
bills could not be legally paid.
j Superintendent Webster took a fling at
the Board of Works, which he said had
I certainly erred In Its figures, and he could
not understand why its estimates had
failed. Webster joined in the recommen
: dation for rejection of the bids.
i As a result of the error in estimates
: the board will be compelled to rent out
: side Quarters for the accommodation of
pupils in the district where the schools
named are located, as the improvements
may have to 'be made while school is in
session.
Silas White, principal of the Spring Val
ley School, asked to be placed on the re
tired list and his application was referred
I to the Rctirement*"Committee.
j The registration of Emma F. Liebold, a
I teacher in the Hancock Grammar School,
was accepted.
A. H. McDonald, principal of the Lin
coln School, recalled his resignation and
if the board agrees he will retain his posi
tion.
Director Denman opposed the applica
tion of Miss Susan Kingsbury, sister of
Deputy Superintendent Kingsbury, for
one year's leave of absence on the ground
that no teacher should get a year's leave
unless she has been in the department
for ten years. The application was
granted.
Leaves of absence were granted to Miss
Ruth G. Campbell. Miss L. C. McNear and
Charles L,. Turner, each for six months.

BOARD OF W ORKS
MAKES ERRORS
The large glass factories and potteries
of New Tork have been victimized to the
extant of about flGOO and several large
fake orders have been given them by an
unknown swindler who has been posing
as F. W. Dohrmann, the well known local
merchant. The swindler's methods have
been simple and easy. His mode of pro
cedure has been to visit the large fac
tories, inquire as to the orders on file
from the Sen Francisco house of which
he was posing as the head, discuss the
business knowingly, augment or vary the
order and incidentally make a borrow of
from $100 to $300. A draft for $300 on an
Eastern bank, bearing the. forged name
of F. W. Dohrmann, made telegrams of in
quiry fly back and forth from the East
ern metropolis to this citj^ and- resulted
in the case being placed in the hands of
the New York police.
Mr. Dohrmann has absolutely no clew
as to who the man can be who appears to
be eo thoroughly conversant with the
business of his firm and the crockery
business In general. No man has been
discharged for the past two years from his
house, and this fact coupled with the in
telligence that the fictitious Dohrmann ap
pears to possess about the intimate busi
ness of the firm makes the case very mys
tifying. Considerable correspondence has
passed between Mr. Dohrmann and the
Eastern firms, who have been swindled,
and this correspondence contains much
interesting matter. One firm complains
of a $2000 order which the glib impostor
gave while he borrowed $300, and
another firm writes pathetically of a car
load of goods shipped at "Mr. Dohr
mann's" order, and which the firm man
aged to stop at Columbus. Ohio.
The description of the swindler which
the Eastern manufacturers have sent out
hero is as follows: "Five feet nine inches
in height, and weighing probably 150
pounds. Dark complexion, black mustache
and hair slightly tinged with gray. Good
teeth and dark eyes. Good appearance,
pleasing address and thoroughly posted
on business affairs of the firm of Nathan,
Dohrmann & Co. Also knows all about
the trade and jobbing and retail prices."

Cyrus Sherman, a teamster, found his

went back to Seiger station, distant a

The big wagon was at a point in thÂ« road
where it could not be passed by tho mail
stage, which came along soon afterward.
Driver Deuel and the passengers decided
to run the clumsy vehicle back to a point
where the road was wider, to allow the
stage to pass. Deuel mounted the lofty
seat to manipulate the brake, and the
passengers were to operate the pole.
The wagon had moved only a few feet
when the brake blocks flew out and it
started down hill. It gained speed with,
every revolution of the wheels. Deuel
was in a serious position, the precipitous
mountain sides offering little inducement
to jump, yet the winding road gave prom
ise of losing the runaway wagon every
second.

SANTA FB, N. Mex., July 10.â There
was almost a riot during the funerals of
Harry Daley and Albino Arias, who were
killed in a duel over a dollar yesterday
morning in the bar of the Oxford Hotel.
The funerals were held within an hour of
each other in the same church, and both
men were burled in Rosario Cemetery.
The duel has stirred up a great deal of
feeling between the Mexican and white
residents of the town. When the long
train of mourners following Arias' body
arrived In the cemetery Daley's grave had
just been closed. Fearing some demon
stration, Daley's friends remained)
grouped about the - fresh mound while
Arias was buried. Hardly was the Mexi
can's body underground when a crowd of
the mourners, inflamed with rage, dashed
toward the party "about Daley's grave,
threatening to dig up Daley's body. .They
tried to. hustle the little group of Ameri
cans away, apparently meaning to carry
out their. threats.
"Drag the corpse of the American pig
through the streets," was their cry.
â¢ Daley's friends rallied and rushed the
Mexicans back. .Peace officers who were
present and officials of the cemetery per
suaded Arias' followers to depart. Fear
ing that the Mexicans may return and try
to disinter the body, Daley's friends have
posted-a guard at his grave. <
The Jact that the revolver with which
Arias is supposed to have shot Daley can
not be " found has given rise . to the sus
picion that a third man shot Daley. . -,

AT THE GRAVE
Drowned in Redwood Creek.
REDWOOD CITY, July 10.â Six-year-old
Walter Barger fell from a boat Into Red
wood Creek last evening and was
drowned. Walter and his sister, a couple
of years older, where playing about some
small sailboats, which were tied to the
wharf. The little girl got tired /and
started home. She had been gone but a
few minutes when she returned and in
quired of some men for her brother. The
latter was nowhere to be seen, but his
hat was on one of the boats, and the men
feared he had fallen in. With a boat
hook they dragged the creek and brought
the body. to the surface.

HOT WEATHER MEALS AND PER-
SONAL COMFORT.
Fruit of some kind should be used at
the breakfast during hot weather. Fol-
low this with a dish of four heaping
teaspoonfuls of Grape-Kuts, cold, and
treated with rich, cold cream. This dish
gives the staying qualities necessary.
Add a slice or two of entire wheat bread,
with a very little butter. Use no meat
for the hot weather breakfast. Let
meat appear but once a day during this
season of the year.
A little care In the selection of food
will help one through the heat of the day
in & way that will not easily be forgotten.

ninety-one and eighty-five one-hundredths
feet (191.85 feet) (measured on a curve) to a
point In the southerly line of Vallejo street;
thence running westerly along the southerly
line of Vallejo street, fifty-seven feet (57 feet)

lejo street with the easterly line of Davis
street; thence running along the easterly line

feet (275 feet) to the" point of beginning; also

Â¦westerly line of East street, thence running

northerly at a right angle to the northerly

westerly line of East street; thence running
southerly along the westerly line of East

terminal freight facilities In connection with

said Board of State Harbor ommlssioners,
its successor or successors. The purposes for

Sergeant Conboy Makes a Raid.
Sergeant Conboy Is still making his
presence felt in Chinatown and is causing
consternation among tho dissolute Celes
tials. Officer Tyrrell of Conboy's squad,
disguised as a soldier, raised a disturb
ance in front of a lottery joint on Jackson
street yestenaay, and the rest of the posse
raided "the place while the guards watched
the antics of the supposed soldier. Seven
players and a -complete outfit were cap
tured through the ruse.

and that he be adjudged guilty of fraud

"Mr. Ashe, the defendant, advised me
to place the jewels in his hands for safe
keeping. Believing what he said was true
and wishing to save the diamonds, I
placed them in the possession of the de
fendant. Subsequently he took the jew
els to New York and later informed me
that he had sold them. Though he ad
mitted having received $15,000 for the jew
els, he only paid into my hands the sum
of $12,000. Since the sale of the jewels the
defendant has refused to make an ac
counting of the sale of the jewels and
upon information and belief this plaintiff
alleges that the defendant either received
$35,000. the value of the jewels, or con
verted a large portion of the same to his
own use."
"On May 24, 1899, R. Porter Ashe, who
was then acting: as my attorney, availed
himself of his relations as my attorney
and represented that certain persons were
seeking to have me declared Incompetent.
He was aware of the fact that I had In
my possession diamonds and jewels val
ued at $35,000, and he Informed me that
the persons who were seeking to have me
declared incompetent would also endeavor
to get possession of the jewels and con
vert them to their own use.

Labor Council Statement.
At the- meeting of the executive com
mittee of the San Francisco Labor Coun
cil held last night the following review of
the local labor situation was adopted:
The Employers' Association, -true to its pol
icy of attempting to disrupt the trades unions,
and to have only servile labor In this city, has
ordered the . wholesale and retail butchers to
Interfere In the cooks' and -waiters' strike. Res
taurants displaying the union card are being
refused meat and threatened that if the card
is not surrendered within a certain data their
meat BUj>Dly will be shut off for a definite or
indefinite period. And the employing- butchers
boast of this action and glory In their power
to rum business men who refuse to obey their
mandates.
The s-Dokesmen of the Employers' Association
have again and again accused the trades unions
or "'dictation. '* or of "trying to run an em
ployer's business." "What should this tyranni
cal exercise of power now displayed by the boss
butchers be called? Is any business man safe
once this policy of Oie wholesalers and jobbers
is established In this city? In view of the well
known fact tliat the agents of the Steel Trust,
the Meat Trust and other trusts are the ruling
spirits of the Employers' Association, commer
cial ruin and vasjtalae-e to the East are the in
evitable results of euch a policy.
The trades unions s=o far are the only parties
fighting openly this commercial feudalism.
How Ions will It take the still Independent
merchants to realize the position they are
thrust into? It can only be hoped for the ben
efit of the city that the aggressive tactics ot
the Employers' Association and its agents will
Quickly arouse to action thÂ» citizens of San
Francisco -who still dare to assert independence.
Leads Girls to Life of Shame.
I>et -us briefly review the cooks' and -wait
ers' fight; its various phases throw a Etrong
light en the tactics of the Employers' Asso
ciation. The cooks and waiters of this city
formed a union to better tuejr conditions. They
were driven to this to save themselves from
moral and physical ruin. Prior to the strike
men and women were compelled to work seven
days a. week from twelve to eighteen hours per
da^ in the restaurants and hotels of this citr.
And this unmerciful grind went on year In
and year out. Every student of human nature
knows the results. Many a strong and virtuous
girl, aftÂ«r a year or so of such labor, lost
her health and with It came moral degenera
tion. The hotels and restaurants of our city
are the best recruiting grounds for the houses
of ill fame. And let society throw no stones at
these unfortunates who fell exhausted by the
Â¦wayside. Society itself t3 responsible for al
lowing these conditions to continue in the ho
tels and restaurants. It Is society's duty to
check the gTeed of Individuals. From the ho
tels and restaurants come also a rich harvest
of male criminals. â¢ It can't be otherwise when
mm are worked thus.
The trades union stepped In and reached out
it* helping hand to the slaves of the dining
room and kitchen. Discouraged by past failures
the response at first vras slow, ' but earnest
and hard work at last gathered about 2800 men
and women under the folds of the Cooks' and
Walters' Alliance. In, due time trade rules
were adopted and presented to the employers
And what were these trade rules? Stripped of
minor details, it was simply a request for one
day's rest in seven (the employers to select the
day of rest) and for a working day of ten
hours for waiters and twelve hours for cooks
The employment of union hÂ»lp and the displav
of the union card were simply methods, used
throughout the length and breadth of our coun
try, to Insure the permanency of union condi
tions once acquired. There was nothing in the
trade rules taking away from the employer the
right to run his business. He still could hire
and discharge whom he pleased, taking his pick
from the 2000 members of the union.

Twenty-Eighth District.
Republicans of the Twenty-eighth As
sembly District, In response to a call is
sued by the Republican Primary League,
met at 46 Steuart street last evening and'
organized a club. Colonel L. P. Crane
was .elected president, M. , M. Foote vice
president and F-. J. Hopper secretary.
Gangs of ruffians and rounders recruited
in the tenderloin sought to disturb the
meeting and caused several fights. This
district is the stronghold of thÂ« bosses,
hence the attempt to prevent organization
of the league forces- The rounders who
provoked the disturbance sought to show
their gratitude to Boss Kelly for favors
extended in keeping them from going to
jail by putting him in nomination for
president of the club. There are many
indications that the league will develop
sufficient strength in the Twenty-eighth
to elect the delegates from that district
to the nominating convention-.

Rely Upon Public Verdict.
"We don't know whether ' the . law can reach
these men who seem determined to ruin the
business of employers that give their employes
humane conditions and who advertise this to
the public by the display of a. union card. We
believe, however, that public opinion -will
quickly and surely render its verdict. We ap
peal to all union men and women and to all
friends of progress and opponents of tyranny
to give their patronage to restaurants dis
playing the union card. We also urge upon all
members of unions and all friend3 of progress
and opponents of tyranny to help us check
these outrages upon the citizens of San Fran
cisco by withholding their patronage from
Hammond & Brod, 727 Market street. These men
are the active and willing tools of the whole
salers. Hammond is the president of the Job
bers' and Retailers';. Association, and boasts
of his success in his interviews in the papers.
The. proprietors of this market were largely
responsible for the strike of the Journeymen
butchers; now they are fighting the cooks and
waiters.
Having thus laid our case before the public,
we ask for its most- careful consideration. We
believe, nay, we know, that the trusts are
trying to crush out the manhood of the people
of this city. We shall â¢ continue the fight
against tyranny and oppression. But the fight
is hot and hard. We ask the support of all
who . believe in progress, of all who believe
in freedom and Justice. We are.

San Francisco Labor Council.

carefully prepared, and the Democrats
will have nothing to do but register the
will of their rulers.

Forty-Fifth District.
The Republicans of the Forty-fotirth
District held a large and most enthusi
astic meeting at Washington Square Hall,
on Stockton and XJnio'n streets, last night
and organized a permanent club for the
district. L. A. Rae called the meeting to
order, making a ringing speech. In which
ho urged Republicans to stand shoulder
to shoulder for party success. He was
unanimously chosen permanent president
of the club, with the following additional
officers:
Forty-Fourth District.
The Forty-third Assembly District Re
publican Club was organized last night at
California Hall. The meeting was
called to order by J. D. Hart, temporary
chairman.- One hundred and fifty voters
signed the roll. On motion of J. H. Har
ney the temporary officers were made per
manent. J. D. Hart resigned as chairman
and Frank P.- Shibeley was elected presi
dent. General R. H. Warfleld and Cap
tain M. R. Roberts were elected vice pres
idents. Other officers chosen were: Secre
tary, Fred Tobelmann; treasurer, Charles
Kauffman. On motion of J. G. Boyne the
executive committee was increased from
fifteen to twenty.
